<p>The primary factor determining diastolic blood pressure is total peripheral resistance.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>SYSTEMIC CIRCULATION (mmHg)</th><th></th><th>PULMONARY CIRCULATION (mmHg)</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Left ventricle</td><td>120/0</td><td>Right ventricle</td><td>25/0</td></tr><tr><td>Aorta</td><td>120/80</td><td>Pulmonary artery</td><td>25/8</td></tr><tr><td>Mean arterial pressure</td><td>93</td><td>Mean pulmonary artery pressure</td><td>15</td></tr><tr><td>Renal glomerular</td><td>50</td><td>Pulmonary capillary</td><td>7-10</td></tr><tr><td>Peripheral veins</td><td>15</td><td>Pulmonary veins</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><td>Right atrium (central venous pressure)</td><td>0-5</td><td>Left atrium</td><td>5-10</td></tr><tr><td>Pressure gradient</td><td>93-0 = 93</td><td>Pressure gradient</td><td>15-5 = 10</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Pressure in the systemic and pulmonary circulation</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="mean-arterial-pressure">Mean Arterial Pressure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mean-arterial-pressure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Mean arterial pressure is the average arterial pressure during the cardiac cycle.</p>
<p>It is calculated as: MAP = cardiac output * total peripheral resistance.</p>
<p>MAP = 2/3rd diastolic pressure + 1/3rd systolic pressure.</p>
<p>If an individual has blood pressure of 120/80 mmHg,</p>
<p>MAP = 2/3rd (80) + 1/3rd (120) = 92 mmHg</p>
<p>During static and high-intensity exercises like weightlifting, a physical compression of blood vessels raises total peripheral resistance. The increase in total peripheral resistance will increase the blood pressure.</p>
<p>Dynamic and aerobic exercises will not significantly affect mean blood pressure (minor isolated systolic rise in blood pressure can be seen) because the decrease in total peripheral resistance (due to dilation of arterioles in exercising muscle) is accompanied by an equivalent increase in cardiac output.</p>
<p>Mean arterial pressure is not equal to mean systemic pressure.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="mean-systemic-pressure">Mean Systemic Pressure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mean-systemic-pressure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Mean systemic pressure is the average pressure that exists in the vascular system if the cardiac output stops and the pressure within the vascular system redistributes.</p>
<p>It is an indicator of how full the circulatory system is.</p>
<p>The value of mean systemic pressure is nearly equal to right atrial pressure - 3-8mmHg.</p>
<p>Right atrial pressure is also called central venous pressure.</p>
<p>Mean systemic pressure depends on the total compliance of the arterial, venous beds and the total blood volume within them.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="compliance-and-elasticity-of-vessels">Compliance and Elasticity of Vessels<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#compliance-and-elasticity-of-vessels" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Compliance means the ability of the vessel to stretch (dilate). If the vessel is easily stretched, it is highly compliant.</p>
<p>Compliance is inversely related to elasticity.</p>
<p>Elasticity is the tendency to rebound back to its original size from a stretch.</p>
<p>A vessel with high elasticity will have low compliance.</p>
<p>Factors affecting compliance:</p>
<p>Endothelial dysfunction reduces compliance due to an increase in arterial stiffness.</p>
<p>This phenomenon is seen in hypertensive, diabetes, and smokers. Atherosclerosis will further aggravate this stiffness.</p>
<p>Pulse contour analysis is a non-invasive method that allows easy measurement of arterial elasticity to identify patients at risk for cardiovascular events.</p>
<p>Age: Newborn will have higher compliant vessels compared to a 50-year-old man.</p>
<p>With the increase in age, compliance will decrease, and elasticity will increase. The less compliant artery will increase the total peripheral resistance that sometimes causes a pseudo-increase in blood pressure during blood pressure measurement in an elderly patient.</p>
<p>Fish oil alters vascular reactivity and favorably influences arterial wall characteristics in patients with non-insulin-dependent diabetes mellitus.</p>
<p>Venous compliance is approximately 25 times larger than arterial compliance and contains nearly 70% of systemic blood volume. Arteries are high-pressure vessels and are very stiff because of the high muscular layer, and therefore, arteries do not represent a significant blood reservoir. The aorta is most compliant in the arterial system.</p>
<p>Compliance is calculated using the following equation, where <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><mi>V</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\Delta V</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">Δ</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.22222em;">V</span></span></span></span></span> is the change in volume, and <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><mi>P</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\Delta P</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord">Δ</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.13889em;">P</span></span></span></span></span> is the change in pressure; <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>C</mi><mo>=</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><mi>V</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><mi>P</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">C = \Delta V / \Delta P</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.07153em;">C</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mord">Δ</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.22222em;">V</span><span class="mord">/Δ</span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.13889em;">P</span></span></span></span></span></p>
<p>Pulse pressure will increase while going distally from the aorta (because compliance decreases). The pedal artery will have higher pulse pressure than the femoral artery, and the femoral artery will have higher pulse pressure than the aorta.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pulse-pressure">Pulse Pressure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pulse-pressure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Pulse pressure is the difference between systolic pressure and diastolic pressure.</p>
<p>It is calculated as:</p>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mtext>systolic&nbsp;pressure</mtext><mo>−</mo><mtext>diastolic&nbsp;pressure</mtext><mi mathvariant="normal">.</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{PP} = \text{systolic pressure} - \text{diastolic pressure}.</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">PP</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">systolic&nbsp;pressure</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">−</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">diastolic&nbsp;pressure</span></span><span class="mord">.</span></span></span></span></span></div>
<p>Other factors that can widen pulse pressure are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Increase in stroke volume (during exercise, systolic pressure increases more than diastolic pressure)</li>
<li>Decrease in vessel compliance (as we grow older, compliance decreases. Therefore, older people have higher pulse pressure compared to younger ones).</li>
</ul>
<p>The compliant artery will have a small pulse pressure, and the stiffened artery will have significant pulse pressure.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-20a6e6cb.jpeg" alt="img-15.jpeg"></p>
<p>Pulse pressure change*</p>
<p>As we go distally from the heart, pulse pressure increases because diastolic blood pressure and compliance of vessels decreases.</p>
<p>This concept is applicable to arteries only because pulse pressure does not take into consideration of venous system.</p>
<p>If you are presented with any vein in multiple choices on an exam, do not select that answer because if calculated, veins will always have lower pulse pressure compared to arteries.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="relationship-between-contractility-preload">Relationship Between Contractility &amp; Preload<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#relationship-between-contractility-preload" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The stroke volume of the heart increases in response to the rise in the volume of blood filling the heart (end diastolic volume) when all other factors remain constant.</p>
<p>The increased volume of blood (preload) stretches the ventricular wall, causing the cardiac muscle to contract more forcefully. The stroke volume may also increase as a result of greater contractility of the cardiac muscle during exercise, independent of the end-diastolic volume.</p>
<p>Performance change with an increase in preload and contractility*</p>
<p>Suppose point A is a normally functioning heart,</p>
<p>When preload is increased (e.g., on laying down), point A will move to point B. This increase in performance is entirely due to preload.</p>
<p>In response to increasing preload, a healthy heart will increase its contractility and shift point B to D. Therefore, an increase in performance from point A to point D is a combined effect of preload and contractility (e.g., exercise).</p>
<p>If contractility is independently increased (e.g., digoxin, dobutamine), it would shift the heart performance from point A to point C. Thus, point A to C is entirely due to an increase in contractility.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-b5a82c0e.jpeg" alt="img-16.jpeg"></p>
<p>Effects of preload, afterload, contractility, and heart rate on cardiac output*</p>
<p>When the line shifts to the left (upward), contractility increases, and when the line shift to the right (downward), contractility decreases.</p>
<p>Various positive inotropic drugs like digoxin, catecholamine, and exercise will cause an increase in contractility.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-3-definitions">Chapter 3. Definitions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-3-definitions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="depolarization-and-repolarization">Depolarization and Repolarization<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#depolarization-and-repolarization" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Cells are electrically stimulated, followed by mechanical contraction of the myocardium.</p>
<p>Return of the cell's membrane potential to a resting state following depolarization. The chambers are relaxed and re-filling in preparation to eject blood during the next contraction.</p>
<p>All cardiac cells are electrically polarized in their resting state, that is, they have a negative resting internal charge with respect to their outsides. This negative resting internal charge is maintained by membrane pumps that ensure sufficient negatively charged ions are kept within the cells.</p>
<p>Depolarization and repolarization refer to the change in the electrical charge. Depolarization is the process in which cardiac cells lose their negative internal charge. Depolarization flows from cell to cell, stripping the cells of their resting polarity, which produces a wave of depolarization that is represented on the EKG.</p>
<p>The cells become repolarized once the electrical conduction has come to an end. During repolarization, the cardiac cells regain their internal negativity, thereby restoring their resting polarity. This electrical activity is also recorded on the EKG.</p>
<p>This electrical activity which passes through the heart is represented on the EKG by the P wave, the QRS complex, and the T wave.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="action-potential">Action Potential<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#action-potential" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Special types of voltage-gated ion channels are embedded in a cell's plasma membrane, which generates an action potential. They handle the generation of electrical impulses in the heart.</p>
<p>Fast response fibers (atrial and ventricular myocardium, His-Purkinje system)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-787417ad.jpeg" alt="img-17.jpeg"></p>
<p>Cardiac action potentials in fast response fibers</p>
<p>Resting membrane potential is the voltage difference between the inside of the membrane and the outside of the membrane.</p>
<p>The intracellular environment of the cell has negative RMP  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mo>−</mo><mn>90</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">V</mi></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(-90\mathrm{mV})</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord">−</span><span class="mord">90</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm" style="margin-right: 0.01389em;">mV</span></span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span></p>
<p>When a cell is appropriately stimulated, positive charges such as sodium and calcium will go into the cell and increase the RMP. When the membrane potential will reach to its threshold  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mo>−</mo><mn>70</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">V</mi></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(-70\mathrm{mV})</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord">−</span><span class="mord">70</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm" style="margin-right: 0.01389em;">mV</span></span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span> , the voltage gated sodium channels open, and a rapid influx of sodium (positive ions) occurs, which will increase the membrane potential to  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo>+</mo><mn>10</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">V</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">+10\mathrm{mV}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7667em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">+</span><span class="mord">10</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm" style="margin-right: 0.01389em;">mV</span></span></span></span></span></span> . This event is called depolarization (phase 0).</p>
<p>Class I antiarrhythmic like procainamide blocks phase 0 in fast response fibers.</p>
<p>Phase 1 is called overshoot and clinically insignificant. Sodium channels are inactivated during this phase. Overshoot develops because of slow potassium current going out of the cell and chlorine coming into the cell. No drugs act on this phase.</p>
<p>After the cell is depolarized, depolarized-sensitive calcium and potassium channels start opening. The level of intracellular potassium (positive charge ion) is higher than extracellular potassium, and the level of extracellular calcium (positive charge ion) is higher than intracellular calcium. For a brief duration, potassium is going out, and calcium is coming in the cell and keep balance in the electromagnetic voltage across the cell membrane (phase 2).</p>
<p>As time passes, a delayed-rectified potassium channel opens, and heavy efflux of potassium occurs from the intracellular to the extracellular environment, and RMP returns back to -90mV (due to concentration-dependent efflux of positive ions). This phenomenon is called repolarization (phase 3).</p>
<p>After the repolarization phase, Na+/K+ ATPase pump will send 2 sodium ions out of the cell in exchange for 3 potassium ions. This will help to increase ion concentration inside and outside the cell (phase 4).</p>
<p>This whole process of depolarization and repolarization is called an action potential. When the cell is undergoing depolarization, some of these sodium ions will go to the next cell via gap junction, and resting membrane potassium reaches a threshold of adjacent cell and leading to its depolarization and repolarization. This cell will stimulate another adjacent cell, and the action potential passes over the entire myocardium.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="action-potential-in-slow-response-fibers-sa-and-av-nodes">Action potential in slow response fibers (SA and AV nodes)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#action-potential-in-slow-response-fibers-sa-and-av-nodes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-7ae819ca.jpeg" alt="img-18.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="cardiac-action-potentials-in-slow-response-fibers">Cardiac action potentials in slow response fibers<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cardiac-action-potentials-in-slow-response-fibers" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Phase 0 (depolarization phase): this phase depends on the calcium channels and not on the sodium channels. With each depolarization, SA node will send signals to contract the heart. Class IV antiarrhythmic like verapamil and diltiazem will slow down this phase.</li>
<li>Phase I &amp; II is not present in SA and AV node</li>
<li>Phase III (repolarization phase): this phase mainly depends on potassium going out of the cell.</li>
<li>Phase IV (raising slope): this phase is also referred to as pacemaker current. It is mainly due to the inward sodium-calcium currents and outward potassium currents. Parasympathetic nervous system, beta-blockers, and calcium channel blockers act on this phase and decrease the heart rate.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="refractory-period">Refractory Period<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#refractory-period" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="refractory-period">REFRACTORY PERIOD<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#refractory-period" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="refractory-period">Refractory Period<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#refractory-period" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>"TETANIZED" CAN'T BE TETANIZED</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-64e236c8.jpeg" alt="img-19.jpeg"></p>
<p>A.R.P. ABSOLUTE REFRACTORY PERIOD</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-c1f72033.jpeg" alt="img-20.jpeg"></p>
<p>A.R.P 200/250MS</p>
<p>SYSTOLIC PHASE</p>
<ul>
<li>"INACTIVATION" OF Na⁺ Ch.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-4-blood-pressure">Chapter 4. Blood Pressure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-4-blood-pressure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hypertension">Hypertension<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hypertension" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>High blood pressure is said to be present if it is persistently at or above 140/90 mmHg in adults. The higher number corresponds to systolic pressure, while lower numbers correspond to diastolic pressure.</p>
<p>Decisions about the aggressiveness of treatment are made according to the classification.</p>
<p>Prehypertension is not considered abnormal, however, advise your patient for lifestyle modifications.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="primary-essential-and-secondary-hypertension">Primary (essential) and secondary hypertension -<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#primary-essential-and-secondary-hypertension" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Primary (essential) hypertension is most common (95% cases). It is diagnosed when there is no identifiable secondary cause.</li>
<li>Secondary hypertension is diagnosed when hypertension is due to some underlying secondary cause (5-8% cases).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pathogenesis">PATHOGENESIS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Systolic Blood Pressure (SBP)</li>
</ol>
<p>SBP correlates with stroke volume and the compliance of the aorta.</p>
<ul>
<li>Stroke volume is directly proportional to the contractility of the heart. An increase in the contractility of the heart will cause an increase in cardiac output.</li>
<li>Compliance of vessels decreases with age because of reduced elasticity of the aorta. The decrease in compliance of arteries is the mechanism for systolic hypertension in an individual &gt;60 years of age.</li>
<li>Systolic blood pressure will rise with an increase in preload, contractility, and a decrease in compliance of the aorta and vice versa.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li>Diastolic Blood Pressure (DBP)</li>
</ol>
<p>DBP correlates with the volume of blood in the aorta during diastole. Diastolic blood pressure will rise with an increase in peripheral vascular resistance, blood viscosity, and heart rate.</p>
<ul>
<li>DBP increases with vasoconstriction of the arterioles due to the increase in the volume of blood present in the artery during diastole.</li>
<li>An increase in blood viscosity is seen in polycythemia, leukemia, dehydration, while a decrease in blood viscosity is seen in anemia, hyper-hydration.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="etiology">Etiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#etiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="primary-hypertension-the-most-common-cause-of-hypertension-is-essential-hypertension-95-cases-of-hypertension">Primary hypertension: the most common cause of hypertension is essential hypertension (95% cases of hypertension).<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#primary-hypertension-the-most-common-cause-of-hypertension-is-essential-hypertension-95-cases-of-hypertension" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Environmental or genetic cause.</li>
<li>Stress: people under stress may overeat or eat a less healthy diet, put off physical activity, drink, smoke, or misuse drugs, which also leads to the release of stress hormones in circulation.</li>
<li>High alcohol intake.</li>
<li>Insulin resistance: common in obesity and is a component of ‘metabolic syndrome’.</li>
<li>Premature baby (low birth weight), maternal smoking and lack of breastfeeding, chewing tobacco, elevated LDL.</li>
<li>Obesity and lack of exercise: excess weight will increase strain on the heart, raises blood cholesterol and triglyceride levels. It will also increase the risk of diabetes. Losing as little as 10 to 20 pounds can help lower your blood pressure and your heart disease risk.</li>
<li>Age: greater than 55 years for men or greater than 65 years for women.</li>
</ul>
<p>Secondary Hypertension: occurs due to pre-existing pathology:</p>
<ol>
<li>Renal disease (3-5% cases)</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Polycystic kidney disease: multiple cysts in the kidney.</li>
<li>Fibromuscular dysplasia (most common in younger females): it is a developmental defect of the blood vessel wall that results in irregular thickening of large and medium-sized arteries and thus causing a stenosis-like condition.</li>
<li>The renal artery is most commonly affected, which will reflexively cause activation of the renin-angiotensinogen system. Bruits can be heard due to renal artery stenosis.</li>
<li>Doppler ultrasound can be used for diagnosing renal artery stenosis. The most accurate is an angiogram. Treatment is renal artery angioplasty and stenting.</li>
<li>Chronic kidney disease: glomerulonephritis, diabetic nephropathy.</li>
<li>Urinary tract obstruction: kidney stones, Proteus infection (struvite stones), congenital malformations, and other conditions that can obstruct the outflow.</li>
<li>The renin-producing tumor will lead to high levels of renin.</li>
<li>It is treated with a combination of a low sodium diet and potassium-sparing diuretic drugs.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li>Endocrine conditions (1-2% cases)</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Cushing’s syndrome: high level of mineralocorticoids.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Hypothyroidism</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pheochromocytoma: tumor of the adrenal medulla which causes an episodic hypertensive crisis.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Neuroblastoma: high level of catecholamine.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>11-hydroxylase deficiency: high level of deoxycorticosterone (acts like aldosterone) and sex hormones (hirsutism).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Acromegaly: increase in growth hormone level in adulthood.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Conn’s syndrome: primary hyperaldosteronism resulting in hypokalemia.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-vascular-conditions">3. Vascular conditions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-vascular-conditions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Increasing age: systolic hypertension due to decreased elasticity of the aorta in the elderly population.</li>
<li>Coarctation of the aorta: narrowing in the arch of the aorta will cause high blood pressure in the upper extremities and low blood pressure in the lower extremities. This results in under perfusion of the kidney resulting in activation of the RAS system.</li>
<li>Vasculitis: inflammation of vessels (increases total peripheral resistance by narrowing vessels).</li>
<li>Collagen vascular disease: occurs when problems with the immune system affect the collagen. This causes arthritis and inflammation of arteries in the tissues that connect joints and other tissues. It can be seen in ankylosing spondylitis (HLA-B27 serotype), dermatomyositis, rheumatoid arthritis (HLA-DR 3, HLA- DR 4 serotype), SLE, and other immune-mediated diseases.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-oral-contraceptives">4. Oral contraceptives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-oral-contraceptives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Activates renin-angiotensinogen system because the hepatic synthesis of angiotensinogen is induced by the estrogen component of oral contraceptives. The best way to manage this cause of hypertension is to stop the use of oral contraceptives, and hypertension goes away in 6 months.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-exogenous-steroids">5. Exogenous steroids<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-exogenous-steroids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Increases blood pressure by volume expansion.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="6-nsaids">6. NSAIDs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-nsaids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Blocks both cyclooxygenase-1 (COX-1) and COX-2 enzymes. COX-2 has a natriuretic effect. The inhibition of COX-2 can inhibit its natriuretic effect. NSAIDs also inhibit the vasodilation effects of prostaglandins at renal afferents and produces vasoconstriction factor (endothelin-1).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="7-smoking">7. Smoking<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#7-smoking" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Causes vasoconstriction and damages arteries leading to atherosclerosis, thromboangiitis obliterans, Raynaud’s phenomenon.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="8-pregnancy">8. Pregnancy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-pregnancy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Gestational hypertension (new onset hypertension that develops after 20° week of pregnancy), pre-eclampsia (hypertension + proteinuria), or eclampsia (hypertension + seizures)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="clinical-presentation">Clinical Presentation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-presentation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Hypertension is usually an asymptomatic condition.</p>
<p>Headache is not a reliable symptom of hypertension. Other findings that might be present in chronic hypertension are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Fatigue, confusion, and vision problems</li>
<li>Chest pain and difficulty breathing</li>
<li>Blood in the urine</li>
<li>Pounding in your chest, neck, or ears</li>
<li>Symptomatic nosebleeds (nose picking is the most common cause of nosebleeds)</li>
</ul>
<p>A variety of symptoms may be indirectly related to hypertension but are not always caused by it, such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>Blood spots in the eyes or subconjunctival hemorrhage, facial flushing, and dizziness</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="complication">Complication<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complication" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Left ventricular hypertrophy (most common overall complication)</li>
<li>Acute myocardial infarction (a most common cause of death)</li>
<li>Atherosclerosis</li>
<li>Intracerebral hematoma (due to rupture of Charcot-Bouchard aneurysms)</li>
<li>Subarachnoid hemorrhage (due to rupture of a berry aneurysm)</li>
<li>Lacunar infarcts (small infarcts due to hyaline arteriolosclerosis). Common location of lacunar infarcts includes basal ganglia, pons, internal capsule, thalamus, and cerebral white matter.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-d722f3a3.jpeg" alt="img-21.jpeg">
Lacunar infarct of putamen*</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-567f8ace.jpeg" alt="img-22.jpeg">
Subarachnoid hemorrhage*</p>
<ul>
<li>Benign nephrosclerosis (atrophy of tubules and sclerosis of glomeruli occurs due to hyaline arteriolosclerosis)</li>
<li>Malignant hypertension (rapid increase in blood pressure accompanied by renal failure and cerebral edema)</li>
<li>Hypertensive retinopathy (arteriovenous nicking, hemorrhage of retinal vessels,</li>
</ul>
<p>retinal infarction, papilledema)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Lifestyle modification is always the first step in the management of hypertension. It is usually recommended for 3-5 months for mild hypertension. Start medical therapy if not controlled.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The goal of hypertension control in diabetic hypertensive or patients under the age of 60 is 140/90 mmHg. The goal of hypertension in age greater than 60 is 150/90 mmHg.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The goal of antihypertensive therapy is the reduction of cardiovascular and renal morbidity and mortality, with the focus on controlling systolic blood pressure. Most patients will achieve diastolic pressure control when systolic blood pressure control is achieved.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Body-mass index (BMI) should be reduced to 25 kg/m² and waist circumferences should be reduced to less than 102 cm in men and less than 88 cm in women. Decreasing the BMI is the most effective way of lifestyle modification and management of hypertension.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The patient is advised to do regular aerobic exercises and follow relaxation techniques like yoga.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Ambulatory blood pressure monitoring should be incorporated.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Diuretics like thiazides are the first line of drug for the management of hypertension, with some exceptions:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Diabetics: ACE inhibitors are the first line of drug</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Benign prostatic hyperplasia: alpha-blockers</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Migraine headache: beta blockers, calcium channel blockers</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Pregnancy: labetalol, methyldopa, hydralazine (for acute reduction)</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If the 2 drugs combination fails to control blood pressure, add a 3rd drug. Suspect secondary hypertension if 2 or 3 drugs fail to control primary hypertension.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Although additional data are needed, renal denervation is a promising therapy in the treatment of resistant hypertension.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-5-notes-on-blood">Chapter 5. Notes on Blood<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-5-notes-on-blood" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="myocardial-ischemia-and-infarction">Myocardial Ischemia and Infarction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#myocardial-ischemia-and-infarction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Ischemia: Ischemia is the restriction in blood supply to the tissues, which results in decreased blood flow and tissue hypoxemia. This decrease in blood flow causes delays in the heart's depolarization and repolarization pattern. If ischemia spreads across the entire thickness of the ventricular wall, there is an imminent danger of myocardial infarction.</p>
<p>Infarction: Infarction is the complete obstruction of one of the coronary arteries, which restricts a tissue's blood supply, thereby causing a lack of depolarization tissue death.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-d35f4933.jpeg" alt="img-23.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="i-using-ekg-to-diagnose-an-evolving-ischemia-and-infarction">I. Using EKG to Diagnose an Evolving Ischemia and Infarction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-using-ekg-to-diagnose-an-evolving-ischemia-and-infarction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There are several stages of ischemia and infarction that can be traced on EKG. In this part, we will cover the three main changes of an evolving infarction that are visible on EKG:</p>
<ol>
<li><span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>T</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">T</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.13889em;">T</span></span></span></span></span>  wave which peaks and then inverts: An inverted T wave is associated with the initial stages of Ischemia.</li>
</ol>
<p>The diagram below shows a normal T wave that is tall and upright in shape. With the onset of infarction, the T wave becomes even taller – a phenomenon which is called ‘peaking.’</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-fd217bde.jpeg" alt="img-24.jpeg"></p>
<p>A couple of hours later, the T wave inverts in the same patient. The diagram below shows an inverted T wave. These changes in T waves are representative of ischemia – a lack of adequate blood flow to the heart muscle.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-142e3606.jpeg" alt="img-25.jpeg"></p>
<p>It is important to note that the inverted T wave is the only representative of ischemia and is not per se diagnostic of infarction. On top of this, there are other conditions that can cause an inverted T wave, such as bundle branch blocks.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>ST segment depression, followed by a ST segment elevation.</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>ST segment depression will be visible on EKG as the ischemia extends into the deeper layers of the myocardium - ST depression is an indicator that an infarction may be imminent.</li>
<li>ST segment elevation reflects myocardial injury. Elevation of the ST segment above the baseline is called the injury pattern and is representative of early-stage infarction. It is suggestive of cellular damage beyond ischemia.</li>
</ul>
<p>The ST segment is the horizontal baseline immediately before the T wave. The diagram below shows a normal ST segment on EKG, which is highlighted by the vertical strip. In general, depression or elevation with a deflection of 2 small boxes or more should be considered clinically significant.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-0dbd2ca6.jpeg" alt="img-26.jpeg"></p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Q wave/the first downward deflection after the P wave is indicative of an infarction.</li>
</ol>
<p>The appearance of new Q waves is an indication of infarction. By this time, the ST segment has usually returned to the baseline.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-78d92447.jpeg" alt="img-27.jpeg"></p>
<p>The new Q waves tend to persist forever in clients who have suffered from myocardial infarction. Scar tissue after myocardial infarction does not depolarize. Because of this, once an area of the myocardium has died, it becomes electronically silent. Electrical vectors will then point away from this area. Any electrodes overlying the dead tissue will record a deep negative deflection. On EKG, therefore, infarctions are presented by negative forces, that is, Q waves and inverted T waves (as highlighted in the diagram above).</p>
<p>Leads that are located near the infarction site will experience an increase in electrical activity that is directed toward them. These leads will record positive and tall R waves. These opposing changes, which are called reciprocal changes, are recorded by distant leads. These distant leads will record a ST segment depression.</p>
<p>On the EKG strip below, the highlighted sections marked with a X show ST segment elevation in Leads I, aVL, and V1 to V5 with reciprocal changes (highlighted and marked with O) in the inferior leads. These EKG findings are indicative of anterior wall myocardial infarction.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-2ea35fb0.jpeg" alt="img-28.jpeg">
II. Normal Vs. Pathologic Q Wave</p>
<p>In normal hearts, a small Q wave can be seen in the left lateral leads and sometimes also in the inferior leads. In comparison, pathologic Q waves, which are indicative of infarction, are wider and deeper in form. Because of this, they are often referred to as significant Q waves. In general, the EKG criteria for pathologic Q waves include the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>A Q wave that is longer than 0.04 seconds in duration (i.e., longer than one small box on EKG paper).</li>
<li>The depth of the Q wave is at least one third the height of the R wave in the same QRS complex.</li>
</ul>
<p>The diagram below shows a pathologic Q wave:</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-384d9116.jpeg" alt="img-29.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="iii-locating-the-infarct">III. Locating the Infarct<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-locating-the-infarct" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>When diagnosing ischemia or infarction, it is very important to follow a pattern: begin by examining the inferior leads, the lateral leads, and then the anterior leads. Examining them in</p>
<p>their groups is important in terms of helping you narrow down the focus of the affected area. Also, recall which main coronary artery supplies this section.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Leads</th><th>Group</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Leads V1, V2, V3, V4</td><td>Anterior chest leads</td></tr><tr><td>Leads I, aVL, V5, V6</td><td>Left lateral leads</td></tr><tr><td>Leads II, III, aVF</td><td>Inferior leads</td></tr><tr><td>Lead aVR</td><td>-</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>It is important to note that if, for example, there is a Q wave in Lead II but not in Lead III and Lead aVF, this should be interpreted as a 'non-specific finding.' It cannot be diagnostic of an old inferior wall myocardial infarction because for a diagnose of ischemia or infarction, there have to be changed in all contiguous leads.</p>
<p>When diagnosing ischemia or infarction, reciprocity is also something that should be kept in mind. The injury itself will be represented by ST elevation. The reciprocal change, therefore, will take the form of ST depression. To help confirm the diagnosis, it is again helpful to examine the leads as individual groups and to note the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Reciprocal changes in the inferior leads are indicative of anterior wall injury and/or later wall injury.</li>
<li>Reciprocal changes in the lateral leads are indicative of inferior wall injury.</li>
<li>Reciprocal changes in the anterior leads are indicative of posterior wall injury.</li>
</ul>
<p>Infarctions are grouped into several categories depending on the site of the infarct and include anterior infarctions, inferior infarctions, lateral infarctions, and posterior infarctions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="anterior-wall-ischemia-infarction">ANTERIOR WALL ISCHEMIA &amp; INFARCTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#anterior-wall-ischemia-infarction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Blood to the anterior myocardium is supplied by the left anterior descending artery. Ischemia affecting this area can be diagnosed by examining the T waves and the ST segments in the precordial chest leads (Leads V1 to V4). The following EKG characteristics are indicative of ischemia affecting the anterior wall:</p>
<ul>
<li>Reciprocal changes in the inferior leads (Leads II, III, and aVF).</li>
<li>Early-stage ischemia is reflected by a T wave inversion in Leads V1 to V4.</li>
<li>Progressing ischemia is reflected by a ST segment depression in Leads V1 to V4.</li>
<li>Ongoing injury is reflected by ST segment elevation in Leads V1 to V4.</li>
<li>Transmural infarction is reflected by Q waves in Leads V1 to V4.</li>
</ul>
<p>The diagram below shows a myocardial infarction (labeled 2) of the anterior wall. This was caused by a blockage (labeled 1) of a branch of the left coronary artery (LCA).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-0ec95e08.jpeg" alt="img-30.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="inferior-wall-ischemia-infarction">INFERIOR WALL ISCHEMIA &amp; INFARCTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#inferior-wall-ischemia-infarction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Blood to the inferior myocardium is supplied by the RCA. An infarction in the inferior wall of the heart is commonly caused by an occlusion of the RCA or its descending branch. Ischemia affecting this area can be diagnosed by examining the T waves and the ST segments in the inferior leads (Leads II, III, and aVF). The following EKG characteristics are indicative of ischemia affecting the inferior wall:</p>
<ul>
<li>Reciprocal changes in the left lateral leads (Leads I, aVL, V5, and V6).</li>
<li>Early-stage ischemia is reflected by a T wave inversion in Leads II, III, and avF.</li>
<li>A progressing ischemia is reflected by a ST segment depression in Leads II, III, and avF.</li>
<li>Ongoing injury is reflected by ST segment elevation in these leads.</li>
<li>Transmural infarction is reflected by Q waves in the same leads.</li>
</ul>
<p>Because the RCA also supplied the AV node, a complete heart block (third-degree heart block) is sometimes also associated with acute inferior wall infarction. A myocardial infarction in the inferior wall will often also result in left axis deviation with the electrical vectors pointing away from the infarct.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="lateral-wall-ischemia-infarction">LATERAL WALL ISCHEMIA &amp; INFARCTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lateral-wall-ischemia-infarction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Blood to the lateral wall is supplied by the left circumflex artery. Ischemia affecting this area can be diagnosed by examining the T waves and the ST segments in the left lateral leads (Leads I, aVL, V5, and V6). The following EKG characteristics are indicative of ischemia affecting the lateral wall:</p>
<ul>
<li>Reciprocal changes in the inferior leads (Leads II, III, and aVF).</li>
<li>Early-stage ischemia is reflected by a T wave inversion in Leads I, aVL, V5, and V6.</li>
<li>Progressing ischemia is reflected by a ST segment depression in Leads I, aVL,</li>
</ul>
<p>V5, and V6.</p>
<ul>
<li>Ongoing injury is reflected by ST segment elevation in these leads.</li>
<li>Transmural infarction is reflected by Q waves in the same leads.</li>
</ul>
<p>A myocardial infarction in the lateral wall will often also result in right axis deviation because electrodes point away from the area of injury (which has become electronically 'silent').</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="posterior-wall-ischemia-infarction">POSTERIOR WALL ISCHEMIA &amp; INFARCTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#posterior-wall-ischemia-infarction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>An infarction in the posterior wall is rarely seen alone. Posterior wall infarction is most observed in the presence of inferior or lateral wall infarction. Blood to the posterior wall is supplied by either the RCA or the left circumflex area (depending on which of the two supplies the majority of the posterior wall). Diagnosing ischemia in this area is significantly more difficult because the placement of the leads in the classic 12-lead EKG does not provide a good view onto the posterior wall of the heart. As a result of this, we have to rely on reciprocal changes in the diagnosis of posterior wall ischemia and infarction.</p>
<p>Depolarization of the posterior wall is the exact opposite of depolarization of the anterior wall. Because of this, diagnosing ischemia affecting the posterior wall is made by looking at the reciprocal changes in the anterior chest leads. Anterior wall infarction can be diagnosed by identifying a ST segment elevation and Q waves in the anterior chest leads (Leads V1, V2, V3, and V4). Ischemia in the posterior wall will produce a 'mirror image'. The following EKG characteristics are indicative of ischemia affecting the posterior wall of the heart:</p>
<ul>
<li>Posterior wall ischemia is reflected by tall R waves and ST segment depression in the anterior leads (Leads V1 to V4).</li>
<li>Progressing ischemia is reflected by the development of larger S waves and smaller R waves in Leads V1 to V4.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="iv-ekg-limitations-sgarbossa-s-criteria">IV. EKG Limitations &amp; Sgarbossa's Criteria<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iv-ekg-limitations-sgarbossa-s-criteria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>As covered above, a typical diagnosis of an evolving myocardial infarction includes ST segment changes as well as the appearance of a new Q wave. An underlying cardiac condition can, however, distort EKG findings, which makes a diagnosis by way of EKG interpretation unreliable. Right bundle branch blocks (RBBBs) do not usually alter EKG findings of ischemia or infarction. Left bundle branch blocks (LBBBs), on the other hand, affect both phases of ventricular depolarization and are thereby represented by Q waves, St segment, the loss of normal R wave progression, as well as T wave changes. All these EKG findings can also be present in ischemia and infarction. Nevertheless, there are specific criteria that can be used to diagnose ischemia or infarction in the presence of LBBB.</p>
<p>Sgarbossa's criteria are a set of EKG findings that can be used to diagnose a suspected infarction in the presence of a LBBBs or ventricular paced rhythms.</p>
<p>It is important to note that although these criteria are sensitive, they are not specific for myocardial infarction. A total score of &gt; 3 is said to have a specificity of 90% for diagnosing infarction.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-843a263c.jpeg" alt="img-31.jpeg">
Concordant ST shifts</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-72cc90e9.jpeg" alt="img-32.jpeg">
New QRS notching</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-d4a5db26.jpeg" alt="img-33.jpeg">
QRS notching and ST elevation &gt; 5 mm</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-6-ekg">Chapter 6. EKG<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-6-ekg" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-is-the-ekg">What is the EKG?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-is-the-ekg" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Basically, electrocardiography (abbreviated EKG or ECG) refers to the tracings of the electrical activities of the heart. With electrocardiography, the clinician can study the electrical activities that go on in the heart. This is done by placing electrodes over the skin. These electrodes can then pick up those small electrical changes generated from the myocardium to the skin surface. It is important to note that this low-intensity current flows through the body, which acts as a volume conductor.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-is-it-for">What Is It For?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-is-it-for" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Electrocardiography helps in the detection of any cardiac abnormalities.</p>
<p>Usually, a 12-lead ECG is used in electrocardiography. The clinician will place 10 electrodes on the limbs of the patient and also on the surface of the chest. By so doing, it becomes possible to capture the direction and the magnitude of the heart’s electrical depolarization at every moment throughout the cardiac cycle. This medical procedure results in the production of a graph – with the voltage on the Y-axis and the time on the X-axis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-are-leads-used-for">What Are Leads Used For?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-are-leads-used-for" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>To record the heart’s electrical activity, electrolytes are placed on the body to measure the information that we receive on EKG. The standard 12-lead EKG comprises of two lead types: chest leads, and limb leads.</p>
<p>Each of these leads has a unique angle (called the angle of orientation) from which it records the heart’s activity. Because of this, it is crucial to be precise when preparing a patient for a 12-lead EKG.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-a8129139.jpeg" alt="img-34.jpeg"></p>
<p>A simple analogy using satellites and cameras may explain how leads function and what they tell us. The following illustration demonstrates this concept.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-bc00a9c1.jpeg" alt="img-35.jpeg"></p>
<p>In this picture, six satellites circle the earth, each labeled with ECG designations. Looking at the picture, what part of the earth is satellite aVL viewing?</p>
<p>What about satellite II?</p>
<p>What about the remaining satellites?</p>
<p>Satellite aVL focuses on Africa, and satellite II focuses on the Atlantic Ocean. aVF views South America, while aVR focuses on the Pacific Ocean.</p>
<p>Let us replace the earth with a heart and satellites with cameras. Just as satellites circle the earth and record activity in specific geographic regions, limb leads encircle the heart and are poised to record the electrical activity in the anatomic areas on which they focus, thereby producing the typical ECG.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-7700e73f.jpeg" alt="img-36.jpeg"></p>
<p>Examine the above cameras (leads). Two "cameras" focus exclusively on the left ventricle: leads</p>
<p>aVL and I. Two leads focus exclusively on the right ventricle: leads aVF and III. Lead II focuses on the apex of the heart that includes a portion of BOTH ventricles.</p>
<p>Notice on the 12 lead ECG, the QRS complex in aVR is inverted because the direction of current is down and to the patient's left. The wave of depolarization is moving away from aVR, a positive terminal, toward the negative end of the aVR axis.</p>
<p>These limb leads to capture the electrical patterns occurring in the ventricles. In doing so, they can identify abnormal electrical patterns on the ECG, such as those produced by ischemia or infarction.</p>
<p>In the frontal plane, limb leads focus on what is occurring in both ventricles. Why ventricles? Because these are the muscular "pumps" that maintain cardiac output. Leads identify electrical abnormalities such as arrhythmia, ischemia, or infarction that may affect cardiac function. For example, if there is a myocardial infarction affecting the left ventricle, the infarction pattern will appear in those limb leads focused on the left ventricle, namely: Leads I, II, and aVL.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-f729d97e.jpeg" alt="img-37.jpeg">
LIMB LEADS FOCUSED ON THE LEFT VENTRICLE</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-4d11fb93.jpeg" alt="img-38.jpeg">
Limb Leads Focused on Left Ventricle I - II - aVI.</p>
<p>Likewise, if there is a myocardial infarction affecting the right ventricle, the infarction pattern will appear in limb leads II, III, and aVF.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-6d4661e0.jpeg" alt="img-39.jpeg">
LIMB LEADS FOCUSED ON THE RIGHT VERNICLE
i</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-e67cbe48.jpeg" alt="img-40.jpeg">
Limb Leads Focused on the Right Ventricle II -III -aVF</p>
<p>What about limb lead aVR? Some authors have referred to aVR as the "forgotten lead" because it does not focus on either ventricle. However, research suggests that elevated ST segments in this lead signify increased mortality in the setting of acute myocardial infarction.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="understanding-chest-leads">Understanding Chest Leads<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#understanding-chest-leads" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Chest leads also referred to as precordial leads, function in the very same manner as limb leads. However, rather than seeing the heart in a frontal plane, these leads view the heart in a cross-sectional plane, i.e., from front to back, as red arrows demonstrate in the following picture.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-80418615.jpeg" alt="img-41.jpeg"></p>
<p>2015-03-17 05 Npatchett, MD</p>
<p>Chest lead "cameras" focus on three specific areas of the heart, namely, the septum (V1-V2), the anterior wall of the left ventricle (V2-V3-V4), and the lateral wall of the left ventricle (V4-V5-V6).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-9994bf63.jpeg" alt="img-42.jpeg"></p>
<p>While the most common cause of complete heart block associated with myocardial infarction is occlusion of the right coronary artery, involvement of the septum in left anterior descending coronary artery infarction is particularly ominous as this affects the bundle of His. Injury to the bundle of His may result in abrupt heart block without warning. For this reason, some advocate placement of an external cardiac pacemaker on standby in managing anterior wall myocardial infarction. Chest leads V2-V3-V4 monitor the "anterior wall" of the heart that houses the septal Bundle of His.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-29fc5121.jpeg" alt="img-43.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="v2-v3-v4-anterior-wall-with-bundle-of-his">V2-V3-V4 - Anterior Wall with Bundle of His<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#v2-v3-v4-anterior-wall-with-bundle-of-his" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Chest leads V4-V5-V6 focus on the lateral wall of the left ventricle, just as do limb leads I- II-aVL. Injury to the left ventricle will demonstrate ischemia and infarction patterns in these leads.</p>
<p>Chest leads focused on the lateral wall of the left ventricle V4-V5-V6</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-3430270f.jpeg" alt="img-44.jpeg"></p>
<p>Realize that leads do not possess the same degree of anatomic precision as angiograms. There may be some degree of overlap, particularly with chest leads. While chest lead V2 focuses on the septum, it also sees a portion of the anterior wall of the left ventricle. Likewise, V4 views both the anterior as well as the lateral wall of the left ventricle. V2 and V4 are transitional leads, focusing on both the anterior wall as well as the lateral wall of the left ventricle.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="contiguous-leads-in-ischemia-and-infarction">Contiguous Leads in Ischemia and Infarction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#contiguous-leads-in-ischemia-and-infarction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Contiguous leads are simply adjacent or adjoining leads. ECG confirmation of either ischemia or infarction must demonstrate characteristic abnormalities in at least two or more contiguous leads. The following diagrams illustrate adjacent, i.e., contiguous, limb and chest lead.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-a989c291.jpeg" alt="img-45.jpeg"></p>
<p>ECG infarction and ischemic patterns should appear in at least two or more adjacent limb and/or chest leads. Appearance in a single lead is suspicious but not diagnostic. Frequently cardiologists refer to such findings as "non-specific changes".</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-7-execution">Chapter 7. Execution<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-7-execution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
