<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ecg-tools">ECG Tools<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ecg-tools" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are certain instruments that help with ECG interpretation:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="calipers">Calipers<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#calipers" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Calipers are perhaps the most important tool in ECG interpretation. Place one pointed edge against one end of what you measure and the other edge at the other end to use one. This position will be maintained by the calipers. You can then move on to a blank area of the ECG strip. Count the number of boxes to get your measurements (example: 1 small box = 0.04 seconds, 1 big box = 0.20 seconds, and so on).</p>
<p>You can use calipers to check whether the distance between complexes is equal. Measure the complex with the two pins. Hold the right pin down and gently swing the left pin to the next complex. If they are the same, the next complex will coincide with the pin. This technique is called "walking", and it is useful for determining complex regularity and detecting ECG abnormalities.</p>
<p>Aside from width, calipers can also measure wave heights. You can likewise "walk" the caliper so that you will know the biggest or smallest complexes and see whether a wave is more positive or more negative.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ecg-ruler">ECG Ruler<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ecg-ruler" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This helps with measuring ECG, but it is not that necessary since a caliper can do what this does.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="axis-wheel-rulers">Axis-wheel Rulers<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#axis-wheel-rulers" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Axis-wheel rulers are used to calculating waves and segments' true axis. This ruler has a red line and a perpendicular arrow.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="straight-edge">Straight edge<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#straight-edge" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It evaluates the baseline and can determine elevations and depressions.</p>
<p>At first, you may be dependent on these instruments, which can enable you to make accurate diagnoses. After a lot of practice, you will be able to quickly make measurements without having to use them.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="calibration">Calibration<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#calibration" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The end of an ECG strip will usually have a calibration box, which is 10 mm high and 0.20 seconds wide. This says that the ECG follows the standard format. This has a rate of 25 mm/sec.</p>
<p>There are ECGs that have a half-standard calibration, especially when the complexes are so large that they overlap. When this is the case, the calibration box will have a stair-like design.</p>
<p>The third calibration is set at 50 mm/sec. The calibration box is 0.40 seconds in width.</p>
<p>It is important to check the calibration of the ECG in order to evaluate the tracings correctly.</p>
<p>These are some of the basic things you need to know before diving into the details of ECG interpretation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="positioning-of-the-patient">Positioning of the Patient<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#positioning-of-the-patient" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The important details are given below.</p>
<ul>
<li>Ensure that electronic devices (e.g., smartphones) are removed from the patient. These devices can produce artifacts (interference) and cause problems with the readings.</li>
<li>Try to put the patient in supine or Semi-Fowler’s position. If the patient cannot tolerate both supine and Semi-Fowler’s position, you can perform the ECG on the patient in a more elevated position.</li>
<li>Ask the patient to relax his shoulders and keep his legs uncrossed with his arms lying flat on his side.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="positioning-of-the-electrodes">Positioning of the Electrodes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#positioning-of-the-electrodes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>It involves the proper placement of limb and chest electrodes at their ideal positions. Interchanging the limb electrodes or mild displacement of chest electrodes causes lead reversal patterns and artifacts in ECG.</p>
<p>The limb electrodes are placed according to their color code, as shown below.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-5c1ef270.jpeg" alt="img-46.jpeg"></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>ELECTRODES</th><th>COLOUR CODE</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>RIGHT ARM</td><td>RED</td></tr><tr><td>LEFT ARM</td><td>YELLOW</td></tr><tr><td>RIGHT LEG</td><td>BLACK</td></tr><tr><td>LEFT LEG</td><td>GREEN</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-c61aea94.jpeg" alt="img-47.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ecg-trace">ECG Trace<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ecg-trace" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-00dcd0c4.jpeg" alt="img-48.jpeg">
ECG Paper</p>
<p>The small squares have a side of  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>1</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">1\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span> . So, the width (x-axis) is  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>1</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">1\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span> , and height (y-axis) is  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>1</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">1\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span> . The large squares have a side of  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>5</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">5\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">5</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span> . So, the width (x-axis) is  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>5</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">5\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">5</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span>  and height (y-axis) is  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>5</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">5\mathrm{mm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">5</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span></span></span></span></span> .</p>
<p>THE WIDTH of THESE SQUARES REPRESENT TIME, and the HEIGHT REPRESENTS AMPLITUDE.</p>
<p>WIDTH</p>
<p>The width of a small square is  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>1</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mn>40</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">1\mathrm{mm} = 40</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">40</span></span></span></span></span>  ms (0.04 seconds)</p>
<p>The width of a large square is  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>5</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mn>200</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">5\mathrm{mm} = 200\mathrm{ms}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">5</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">200</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">ms</span></span></span></span></span></span>  (0.2 seconds)</p>
<p>HEIGHT</p>
<p>The height of a small square is  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>1</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mn>0.1</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">v</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">1\mathrm{mm} = 0.1\mathrm{mv}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">0.1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm" style="margin-right: 0.01389em;">mv</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>
<p>The height of a large square is  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>5</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mn>0.5</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">v</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">5\mathrm{mm} = 0.5\mathrm{mv}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">5</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">0.5</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm" style="margin-right: 0.01389em;">mv</span></span></span></span></span></span></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="speed">Speed<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#speed" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-0583619e.jpeg" alt="img-49.jpeg"></p>
<p>The usual speed Of the ECG paper is  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>25</mn><mtext>&nbsp;</mtext><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">c</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">o</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">n</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">25~\mathrm{mm / second}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mord">25</span><span class="mspace nobreak">&nbsp;</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm/second</span></span></span></span></span></span> . It can be altered in an ECG machine manually. In cases of severe Tachycardia, the speed of ECG paper is increased to  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>50</mn><mtext>&nbsp;</mtext><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">c</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">o</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">n</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">50~\mathrm{mm / second}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mord">50</span><span class="mspace nobreak">&nbsp;</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm/second</span></span></span></span></span></span> . As paper moves faster, the distance between two complexes increases, helping in better recognition of ECG patterns and waves.</p>
<p>WIDTH</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>1 small square</th><th>40 ms</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>1 large square</td><td>200 ms</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>HEIGHT</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>1 small square</th><th>0.1 mV</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>1 large square</td><td>0.5 mV</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Often, we use the term box instead of squares, and both are used interchangeably.</p>
<p>Where are we supposed to look first when we are given an ECG?</p>
<p>The first and the foremost thing to look at in the ECG is its Standardization, which is indicated by the Symbol. In a standardized ECG, the height and width of the complexes are precise. However, in an unstandardized ECG, they may vary in size and shape.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="standardization">Standardization<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#standardization" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In simpler terms, standardization is considered a quality indicator for ECGs.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-ce1370f0.jpeg" alt="img-50.jpeg"></p>
<p>The amplitude of standardization is  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>10</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mn>10</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10\mathrm{mm} = 10</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">10</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">10</span></span></span></span></span>  small squares  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo>=</mo><mn>1</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">v</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">= 1\mathrm{mv}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.3669em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm" style="margin-right: 0.01389em;">mv</span></span></span></span></span></span>  along the Y-axis. The width of the standardization is  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>5</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mn>5</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">5\mathrm{mm} = 5</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">5</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">mm</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">5</span></span></span></span></span>  small squares  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo>=</mo><mn>200</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">= 200\mathrm{ms}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.3669em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">200</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">ms</span></span></span></span></span></span>  along X-axis.</p>
<p>If the ECG is not properly standardized on the first instance it should be repeated properly standardized one to avoid erroneous reporting.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th>Normal Standardized ECG Amplitude &amp; Duration of the complex normal</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td>Under Standardized ECG Amplitude decreased</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-71c8a9f5.jpeg" alt="img-51.jpeg"></p>
<p>NOTE
As we use thermal paper for recording ECG’s, the tracings may fade off after a few months. So, it is advisable to have a soft copy or photocopy for future reference and medicolegal aspects.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-1ca413be.jpeg" alt="img-52.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-213dcbf7.jpeg" alt="img-53.jpeg">
Cardiac Action Potential</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>EKG: Waves and Intervals</th><th></th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>■ P wave = depolarization of the atria</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ QRS = depolarization of the ventricle</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ T wave = repolarization of the ventricle</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Normal Values</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Duration (horizontal axis)</td><td></td><td>Height (vertical axis)</td></tr><tr><td>P wave</td><td>&lt;0.12 s</td><td></td><td>&lt;2.5 mm</td></tr><tr><td>P-R interval</td><td>0.12-0.20 s</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>QRS interval</td><td>0.08-0.10 s</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>QT interval</td><td>0.35-0.44 s</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>QTc interval = QT interval divided by the square root of R-R interval</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Age group</td><td></td><td>QTc interval by age</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>0-2 yrs</td><td></td><td>0.37-0.53</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>2-10 yrs</td><td></td><td>0.39-0.42</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>10-14 yrs</td><td></td><td>0.40-0.42</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>&gt;15 yrs</td><td></td><td>0.35-0.44</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-8-the-ecg-complex">Chapter 8. The ECG Complex<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-8-the-ecg-complex" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-9416c080.jpeg" alt="img-54.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="components-of-the-ecg-complex">Components of the ECG complex<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#components-of-the-ecg-complex" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The ECG records waves, intervals, and segments. These have particular morphologies (forms), axes, durations, and amplitudes when normal. When the ECG readings are different from these traits, there may be something wrong with the heart.</p>
<p>A wave is a deflection from a baseline, which is the line from one TP segment to another one next to it. It can be single, positive, negative, biphasic, isolated, or have multiple parts that can be positive or negative.</p>
<p>An interval is the time distance between two cardiac events. A segment is a specific part of a complex on an ECG. A segment is different from an interval.</p>
<p>This is the normal sequence of the waves:</p>
<p>Tall waves are given big letters, while small waves are labeled with small letters.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prime-waves">Prime waves<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prime-waves" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Waves are called prime when they cross the baseline or change directions.</p>
<p>When there are extra waves where they aren't supposed to be, they are labeled as prime waves.</p>
<p>A wave can be a double prime when it occurs twice.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-p-wave">The P Wave<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-p-wave" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The P wave is normally the first wave on the TP segment. It represents the atria's electrical depolarization. The wave begins when the SA node fires, and it embodies the impulse transmission through the internodal pathways, the Bachmann bundle, and the atrial myocytes.</p>
<p>Since the P wave represents atrial depolarization, P wave abnormalities signify atrial abnormalities. Check the inferior leads (II, III, and aVF), and precordial lead V1 for this.</p>
<p>The wave duration is between 0.08 – 0.11 seconds or less than 120 ms. Its axis is commonly directed downwards and to the left (Axis: 0 to 75 degrees) since it goes through the atrioventricular node and atrial appendages.</p>
<p>The first one third of the wave embodies the right atrial activation, the last third represents the left atrial activation, and the middle is the blend between the two.</p>
<p>P waves are normally upright in leads I and II and inverted in lead aVR. They are monophasic in lead II and biphasic in V1.</p>
<p>In V1, the P wave is biphasic and begins with a positive deflection, showing right atrial activation, and ends with a negative deflection, which reflects left atrial activation. Because of this, the state of each individual atrium can be determined by looking at each part of this waveform.</p>
<p>These are some things that can be known from P waves:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-tp-wave">The TP Wave<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-tp-wave" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The TP wave symbolizes the repolarization of the atria. This moves in the opposite direction of the P wave.</p>
<p>This is not commonly obvious because it coincides with the more prominent QRS wave.</p>
<p>The TP wave appears when the QRS is absent, such as in the case of AV dissociation or non-conducted beats. This also shows up in PR depression or in the ST segment depression during sinus tachycardia.</p>
<p>It shows as ST depression because the QRS arrives earlier, and the TP wave draws the ST segment down.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-pr-segment">The PR Segment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-pr-segment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The PR segment is normally lying along the baseline but can be depressed or elevated by up to 0.8 mm. PR segment abnormalities indicate atrial infarctions/ischemia and pericarditis.</p>
<p>The PR segment shows the transmission of the electrical depolarization impulse through the AV node, a bundle of His, bundle branches, and the Purkinje system.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-pr-interval">The PR Interval<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-pr-interval" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The PR interval involves events from the start of the electrical impulse in the SA node to ventricular depolarization. (Beginning of impulse, atrial depolarization, and repolarization, stimulation of AV node, His bundle, bundle branch, and Purkinje System).</p>
<p>This normally lasts from 0.12 – 0.20 seconds.</p>
<p>If it's longer than 0.20 seconds, it is a sign of a first-degree AV block.</p>
<p>It can also be called the PQ interval when a Q wave begins the QRS complex.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-1826f29d.jpeg" alt="img-55.jpeg">
The QRS Complex</p>
<p>The QRS complex reflects ventricular depolarization. It usually lasts 0.06 – 0.11 seconds with an axis of -30 to +105 degrees, going downward and to the left.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="q-wave">Q Wave<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#q-wave" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A Q wave is considered significant if it lasts for 0.03 seconds or longer or if it is as tall or taller than 1/3 of the R wave. When these conditions are present, there is a myocardial infarction (MI). Insignificant Q waves are traditionally found in I, aVl, and V6, and these are caused by septal innervations. These are usually called septal Qs.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="intrinsicoid-deflection">Intrinsicoid Deflection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#intrinsicoid-deflection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This begins from the start of the QRS complex to the start of the downward slope of the R wave, in leads that begin with an R wave and do not have a Q wave. It symbolizes the time taken for the electrical impulse to traverse the Purkinje system from the inner layer to the outer layer of the heart.</p>
<p>It is shorter in the right precordial leads (V1-V2) because the right ventricle is thinner than the left.</p>
<p>The intrinsicoid deflection will be longer if the myocardium is thicker than normal (e.g., Ventricular hypertrophy), or when the electrical system conducts slower in that area because of a delay (e.g. Left bundle branch block).</p>
<p>These are the normal upper limits for intrinsicoid deflection:</p>
<p>Left precordial = 0.045 seconds</p>
<p>Right precordial = 0.035 seconds</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-st-segment">The ST Segment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-st-segment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The ST segment is the electrically neutral period in between ventricular depolarization and</p>
<p>repolarization. In this phase, the myocardium is maintaining its contraction to push the blood from the ventricles. Its axis is normally inferior and to the left.</p>
<p>This is the section from the QRS complex's end to the T wave's start. It is usually found on the baseline but can deviate up to 1 mm from it in normal patients' limb leads and 3 mm in the right precordial. This may be caused by left ventricular hypertrophy or an early repolarization pattern.</p>
<p>The J point marks the spot where the QRS complex stops and the ST segment begins.</p>
<p>When the ST segment is elevated, it should be considered as significant, as it can reveal a myocardial infarction or injury.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-t-wave">The T Wave<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-t-wave" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The T wave signifies ventricular repolarization. This is the next positive or negative deflection after the ST segment. It is expected to begin in the same direction as that of the QRS complex. The axis is downward and to the left for this wave as well.</p>
<p>The T wave is asymmetrical, with the first part dropping or rising slowly and the last part moving rapidly.</p>
<p>To check whether the T wave is symmetrical or not, draw a perpendicular line from the wave's peak to the baseline and compare the two sides.</p>
<p>Symmetric Ts usually signify problems.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-qt-interval">The QT Interval<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-qt-interval" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The QT interval comprises the QRS complex, ST segment, and T wave.</p>
<p>It involves cardiac events of ventricular systole, that is, from the start of ventricular depolarization until the end of the repolarization cycle.</p>
<p>The QT interval depends on the patient's age, sex, heart rate, and electrolyte condition.</p>
<p>A prolonged QT can mean arrhythmias.</p>
<p>To obtain QTc, add QT + 1.75 * (ventricular rate -60)</p>
<p>The QTc interval normally lasts 0.410 seconds, and anything longer than .419 is considered prolonged.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-u-wave">The U Wave<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-u-wave" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The U wave is the small wave commonly seen after the T wave and before the following P wave. This has low voltage and has the same direction as the T wave.</p>
<p>It is not clear as to what it symbolizes, but it is theorized that it stands for ventricular depolarization and endocardial repolarization.</p>
<p>It is seen in normal individuals, especially those with bradycardia (slow heartbeats). It is also evident in hypokalemia or low potassium in the blood, thus there is no hyperkalemia (high levels of potassium) when a U wave exists.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-r-r-interval">The R-R Interval<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-r-r-interval" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The R-R interval is the distance between two identical points or peaks of two consecutive QRS complexes.</p>
<p>This is used to determine whether the rhythm is regular or irregular. Those with regular rhythms</p>
<p>have consistent R-R intervals.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-p-p-interval">The P-P Interval<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-p-p-interval" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is the distance between two identical points on a P wave and the next one. This is used to assess rhythm abnormalities such as atrial flutter, Wenckeback second-degree heart block, and third-degree heart block.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="baseline">Baseline<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#baseline" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The baseline of the ECG is the line from the TP of one complex to the TP of another one. The PR segment should be on this line, but there are times when it is elevated.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="einthoven-s-triangle">Einthoven's Triangle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#einthoven-s-triangle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Willem Einthoven (1860-1927) was a Dutch doctor, a physiologist who developed the technology for producing clinically useful ECG recordings and understanding of biophysics. He received the Nobel Prize in Medicine for doing so.</p>
<p>Beginning at the SA node, the wave of depolarization spreads downward diagonally from right to left, as pictured. ECG leads viewing the approaching depolarization wave will have upright QRS-T wave complexes (aVL, I, II, aVF, III). The QRS-T wave complex in aVR will normally appear inverted since that lead sees the depolarization wave moving away.</p>
<p>Roman numerals and letters designate limb leads I - II - III - aVR (augmented vector right) - aVL (augmented vector left) / aVF (augmented vector foot). In addition to limb leads, six skin electrodes are attached to the chest. Four limb leads and six chest leads produce the 12 lead ECG tracing. Augmented vector terminology refers to directional electrical forces at play during the cardiac cycle.</p>
<p>Chest leads are designated V1-V2-V3-V4-V5-V6. The following picture shows the placement of chest leads. These leads view the heart in the horizontal plane from front to back.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-84d1a90c.jpeg" alt="img-56.jpeg"></p>
<p>Keep in mind that this arrangement of skin electrode placement is typical for a standard 12 lead ECG. However, certain clinical situations may warrant repositioning electrodes to evaluate other cardiac abnormalities. For example, when a patient suffers blockage of the right coronary artery and subsequent right ventricular infarction, position chest electrodes on the right side of the chest to demonstrate more clearly right ventricular STEMI. To evaluate a posterior wall myocardial infarction that may be easily missed, place electrodes on the back of the chest to reveal characteristic ST segment elevation not seen in anterior chest leads.</p>
<p>Interpreting the ECG takes some practice because there's a lot going on in the graph. It is important that you understand everything you read here. You don't have to memorize everything, but do keep notes or this book handy.</p>
<p>When you have an ECG to interpret, look at it broadly and take note of any outstanding features. Do not dwell on the tiny details at this point but try to form a general impression about it. Does it</p>
<p>seem like a case of arrhythmia, ischemia, etc.? Keep your tentative conclusion in mind as you go through the next steps.</p>
<p>An ECG gives a lot of information that is only unlocked if you know what each part of the tracings means. In general, a normal reading has the following characteristics:</p>
<p>Reading is considered abnormal when these are present:</p>
<p>After gaining an overview, go over the ECG sequentially. Determine where the normal and abnormal beats are. For the normal ones, evaluate the axis, intervals, blocks, and other things you need to find out. For the abnormal beats, think about what may be causing them.</p>
<p>Before anything else, you must know that the above features are just general indicators of normality and abnormality. If you want to get useful insights, you have to consider other features. Read on to know how to get specific information from ECG readings.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-4532518e.jpeg" alt="img-57.jpeg">
Rate</p>
<p>There are several ways the rate can be determined. The most often used is to count the number of large squares between subsequent QRS complexes and divide 300 by this number. This works because there are 60,000 milliseconds in a minute, and each large square represents 200 milliseconds. 60,000 divided by 200 gives 300. Thus, if there were only one large square between two subsequent QRS complexes, the rate would be 300 beats per minute. If there were two, the rate would be 150 beats per minute, and so on.</p>
<p>This method is valid only if the rhythm is regular. In a like manner, a more precise rate can be determined by dividing the number of small squares into 1500. This works because there are five small squares for each large square (3 x 5 = 1500). Again, this method is valid only if the rhythm is regular. When the rhythm is irregular, it is better to use more than two QRS complexes to determine the rate. The easiest method is to count the number of intervals in a six second time period and multiply by ten to obtain the heart rate in beats per minute.</p>
<p>Many monitoring systems display a six second sweep to make use of this technique. On an EKG, there are usually three second markers.</p>
<p>The number of QRS complexes between two of these multiplied by ten gives the heart rate in beats per minute.</p>
<p>The ECG is traditionally 10 seconds long, with each lead covering 2.5 seconds. The paper has three to four strips. The top three strips have 12 leads.</p>
<p>Bear in mind that the vertical height of a segment or wave is measured by millimeters, while the width is measured in milliseconds. This is because the width of the tracing reveals the duration of the electrical activity, while the height shows its voltage.</p>
<p>Some ECG devices calculate the heart rate on their own, but they may be inaccurate, especially when the waveforms are abnormal. It is always better to know how to make calculations manually.</p>
<p>There are many ways to compute the rate of the heartbeat:</p>
<p>Check: is the heart rate faster or slower than expected? Again, the normal heart rate is 60-100 beats per minute, but this may vary according to the patient's age and other health conditions.</p>
<p>You can already make a diagnosis based on heart rate:</p>
<p>For adults, the normal rate is at 60 – 100 beats per minute (BPM). If the rate is greater than 100 BPM, tachycardia is present. If it is less than 60 BPM, there is bradycardia.</p>
<p>Children normally have faster heart rates than adults because of their smaller body sizes. Anything faster than the values given below counts as tachycardia; if slower, then it is bradycardia.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="rhythm">Rhythm<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rhythm" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The heartbeat rhythm is more easily studied by using a rhythm strip, which is most often a 10 second recording from Lead II (if you used a 12 lead ECG). Look into the other leads as well to make a more accurate diagnosis about the rhythm.</p>
<p>One of the easiest things to determine from the rhythm is whether it is slow or fast (bradycardia or tachycardia), and whether it's irregular or regular.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="measuring-rhythm">Measuring Rhythm<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#measuring-rhythm" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are two ways to measure rhythm: <strong>Using a piece of paper and pencil</strong>: Place the paper along the baseline. Move it up so that the edge is near the R wave’s peak. On your paper, mark the R waves of two consecutive QRS complexes to get the R-R interval. Transfer the paper across the ECG tracing and see whether the following R-R intervals line up with your marks. If yes, you can say that the ventricular rhythm is regular. If not, it is irregular. Do the same for the P waves (P-P intervals), to know whether the heart’s atrial rhythm is regular or irregular.</p>
<p><strong>Using calipers</strong>: Place one point of your caliper on the peak of an R wave. Adjust the calipers so that the other point lands on the next R wave. This gives you the R-R interval. Swivel the calipers to check whether it falls on the third R wave’s peak. If they do, the R-R (ventricular) rhythm is regular. If not, it is irregular. You can also determine the atrial rhythm this way by measuring the P-P interval.</p>
<p>If the heartbeat has a regular rhythm, count how many complexes there are on the rhythm strip. This is usually 10 seconds long. Multiply 6 by this number to get the average number of complexes per minute.</p>
<p>An irregular heart rhythm may be regularly irregular, with a recurring pattern of irregularity, or irregularly irregular, which means that the rhythm is totally disorganized.</p>
<p>Is the rhythm grouped or ungrouped? Abnormal heart rhythms have grouped beats. Check whether the rhythm is wide or narrow.</p>
<p>This means observing the average width of the QRS complexes.</p>
<p>If it is wide, there may be a conduction problem coming from the ventricles or from the supraventricular region (above the ventricles). If narrow, the abnormality may exist in the sinus node, atria, or junctional region.</p>
<p>Look for P waves – if you can’t find them, the patient may be experiencing atrial fibrillation or sinus arrest.</p>
<p>If they are present, check the ventricular and atrial rate. Are all P waves similar to each other? They are expected to be the same if there is a 1:1 conduction to the QRS complexes.</p>
<p>Abnormal ratios between P waves and QRS complexes may indicate atrioventricular dissociation (AV dissociation). In complete AV dissociation, the atrial and ventricular electrical activities always occur separately. In incomplete AV dissociation, capture beats show up infrequently.</p>
<p>If there is an abnormality in P wave shape and PR interval, this suggests that there is something wrong with the conduction of the sinus, atria, junction, or ventricles. You will know the location depending on which leads the abnormalities show up.</p>
<p>If the heartbeat is irregular, compute for the range. Inspect the PR, QRS, QTc, PP, and RR intervals. Observe if there are irregularities in the intervals.</p>
<p>Consider onsets and terminations. If they are abrupt, a re-entrant process may be causing them. If gradual, it is possible that an area of the heart has increased automaticity (ability to conduct impulses). There are a lot of heart problems that can be diagnosed by heart rhythm type:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="bradycardia">Bradycardia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bradycardia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-e92c9c10.jpeg" alt="img-58.jpeg"></p>
<p>Sinus Bradycardia: Note that the rate is about 45 bpm.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-908f2b1f.jpeg" alt="img-59.jpeg">
Sinus Tachycardia: Note that the rate is more than 100 bpm.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="axis">Axis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#axis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Heart axis</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-43bdd202.jpeg" alt="img-60.jpeg"></p>
<p>The electrical impulses are vectors that have energy and direction. Vectors add up when they head towards the same direction, decrease in energy and change their direction when they are going in opposite locations, and add or subtract energy and change directions when they meet at an angle. The heart has numerous vectors, but as mentioned, they all combine to produce major currents. All these vectors together are referred to as the electrical axis.</p>
<p>There are a number of vectors detected by the ECG's electrodes: The P-wave vector, T-wave vector, ST segment vector, and QRS vector.</p>
<p>Electrodes pick up electrical activity. When a positive electrical impulse moves away from an electrode, the ECG registers it as a negative or downward wave. When a positive wave approaches an electrode, the ECG transforms it into a positive or upward wave. When the electrode sits in the middle of a wave, the ECG demonstrates a positive wave for the energy coming towards it and a negative wave for the energy retreating from it.</p>
<p>These electrodes are placed at specific angles to the main axis to get a three-dimensional view of the heart's electrical activity.</p>
<p>Just like with heart rate and rhythm, the electrical axis is affected by abnormalities. This is why the electrical axis is studied in the ECG.</p>
<p>As you may recall, the QRS complex stands for ventricular depolarization. This is the strongest impulse in the heart, and thus the QRS complex determines the heart's main axis.</p>
<p>The degree of the QRS axis will tell you whether it is normal or deviated:</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-6336c78c.jpeg" alt="img-61.jpeg"></p>
<p>Normal: between -30 to +90 degrees</p>
<p>These are some ways to determine the axis:</p>
<p>The quadrant method involves looking at leads I and aVF.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Lead I</th><th>Lead aVF</th><th>Quadrant</th><th>Axis</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Positive</td><td>Positive</td><td>Left Lower</td><td>Normal</td></tr><tr><td>Positive</td><td>Negative</td><td>Left Upper</td><td>Possible LAD</td></tr><tr><td>Negative</td><td>Positive</td><td>Right Lower</td><td>RAD</td></tr><tr><td>Negative</td><td>Negative</td><td>Right Upper</td><td>EAD</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The Isoelectric Lead Method is more precise than the method above and involves finding the isoelectric/equiphasic lead. This is the frontal lead that has zero net amplitude, meaning a QRS that is a flat line or biphasic, with R wave height that is equal to Q or S wave depth.</p>
<p>After finding the isoelectric lead, the next step is to find the positive leads – those with the highest R waves or biggest R to S ratios.</p>
<p>When this is done, the QRS axis is calculated by going 90 degrees to the isoelectric lead and pointing towards the positive leads.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-9-how-to-read-an-ecg-trace">Chapter 9. How to Read an ECG Trace<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-9-how-to-read-an-ecg-trace" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="steps-in-ecg-interpretation">Steps in ECG Interpretation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#steps-in-ecg-interpretation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Determine the rhythm and regularity</li>
<li>Calculate the rate</li>
<li>Evaluate P wave</li>
<li>Calculate PR interval</li>
<li>Analyze QRS complex</li>
<li>Examine T wave</li>
<li>Calculate QT interval</li>
<li>Look for other characteristics</li>
</ol>
<p>Check the heart rate. Is it fast (tachycardia &gt;100 bpm) or slow (bradycardia &lt;60 bpm)? What is the rate? (calculate the rate by dividing 300 by the number of big squares between one R-R interval).</p>
<p>Check the rhythm. Is it regular or regularly irregular (i.e., a pattern to the irregularity) or irregularly irregular (no pattern – AF). If unsure of rhythm, place a piece of paper along with the ECG and mark the positions of 3 successive R waves. Move the paper along to see if the R waves on the paper match up with successive R waves on the ECG (if so, then it is regular).</p>
<p>Are there P waves? P waves normally precede each QRS complex. Normal length 0.12 – 0.2s or 3-5 small squares. Absent P waves can indicate AF or sinoatrial block. A disconnection between P waves and QRS can indicate heart block.</p>
<p>Does a QRS complex follow each P wave? Check duration and voltage (height). Normal duration &lt;0.12s or 3 small squares. &gt;0.12s can indicate ventricular conduction disorders such as bundle branch block. Large (tall) QRS complexes can indicate ventricular enlargement.</p>
<p>Check the ST segment. Is it on the isoelectric line? Is it elevated or depressed?</p>
<p>Check the QT interval. Measure from the start of QRS to the end of T wave. (QTc &lt;0.44s).</p>
<p>Check the T waves. Normal T wave is inverted in aVR, V1, and sometimes V2. It is abnormal if the T wave is inverted in I, II, and V4-6. It can be “peaked/tented” in hyperkalemia and flattened in hypokalemia.</p>
<p>Check for extra beats. Extra beats may be found in abnormal heart rhythms. Ectopic beats can be a normal finding in some patients.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="steps-in-12-lead-ecg-interpretation">Steps in 12 lead ECG Interpretation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#steps-in-12-lead-ecg-interpretation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<li>Axis</li>
<li>Bundle branch block</li>
<li>Enlargement of chamber</li>
<li>Ischemia or infarction</li>
<li>Other abnormalities</li>
</ol>
