pattern</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>R in aVL plus S in V3 &gt;28 mm in men and &gt;20 mm in</td><td>Cornell criteria</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table><thead><tr><th>Summary of 12 lead EKG Interpretations- Cont...</th><th></th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Ischemia</td><td>ST segment depression and T wave inversion</td><td>Lead I, aVL, V5 and V6</td><td>Lateral wall (Left circumflex artery)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Lead II, III and aVF</td><td>Inferior leads (Right coronary artery)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Lead V1, V2, V3 and V4</td><td>Anterior wall (LAD territory)</td></tr><tr><td>Infarction</td><td>Acute myocardial infarction</td><td>ST segment elevation in the target area with ST segment depression and T wave inversion in the opposite area</td><td>Reciprocal changes</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Old myocardial infarction</td><td>Presence of large Q waves in target areas</td><td>At least &gt; 1 mV</td></tr><tr><td>Other abnormalities</td><td>Pulmonary embolism</td><td>Prominent S in lead I, Q wave and inverted T wave in lead III</td><td>S1Q3T3 pattern</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Right ventricular strain pattern</td><td>ST depression in V1-V3</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Sinus tachycardia</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>New incomplete RBBB</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Hyperkalemia (depending on serum level)</td><td>Tall peaked T waves</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>ST segment depression</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Various bundle branch block</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Severe bradycardia with AV block</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>V tach/V-Fib</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Pericarditis</td><td>PR segment depression</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Generalized ST segment elevation</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Hypocalcaemia</td><td>prolonged QTc</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Flat or inverted T waves</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Prolonged ST segment without increase in T wave duration</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Hypercalcemia</td><td>Short QTc</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>PR segment prolongation</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Hypomagnesaemia</td><td>Peak T wave</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Prominent T wave</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>prolonged QRS</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>ST segment depression</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Polymorphic ventricular tachycardia</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Pericardial effusion or Cardiac tamponade</td><td>Low voltage EKG</td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Electrical alternance</td><td>Beat to beat change in amplitude</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-10-how-to-determine-heart-rate">Chapter 10. How to Determine Heart Rate<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-10-how-to-determine-heart-rate" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>When interpreting the EKG, it is crucial to have a systematic approach. The five main areas you need to master in order to effectively interpret EKG are: rate, rhythm, axis, block, and infarction. In this section, we will cover how to calculate the heart rate.</p>
<p>The heart rate is easily calculated from the EKG using this simple three-step method. Firstly, however, it is important to remember that one small square on EKG paper represents 0.04 seconds and one large square (made up of five small squares) represents 0.2 seconds.</p>
<p>Calculate the heart rate by:</p>
<ol>
<li>Finding an R wave that falls on a heavy line that comprises a large box.</li>
<li>Then count until the next R wave, the number of large squares.</li>
<li>Finally, the boxes are counted, and the rate is determined accordingly by beats per minute (bpm).</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-4d70481b.jpeg" alt="img-62.jpeg"></p>
<p>If the next R wave falls on the third thick line, the patient's heartbeat would be 100bpm. A heart rate between 60 and 100bpm with an upright P wave indicates that the impulse is being generated from the sinus code. If the next R wave does not fall on a thick black line, you can accurately estimate the heart rate by counting the small boxes and remembering that each small box represents 0.04 seconds.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-93376a26.jpeg" alt="img-63.jpeg"></p>
<p>By determining the bpm, you will also be able to identify the focus of the beat. Although every cell in the heart can technically initiate action and stimulate the heartbeat, only a few areas do so on a regular basis: the sinus node, the AV node, and the ventricles. The sinus node is the fastest focus.</p>
<p>However, the slower impulse will always be overdriven by the fastest pacemaker that sets the pace.</p>
<p>Note also that impulses that are generated from the AV node or from the ventricles will not have a P wave as the presence of the P wave indicates sinus node activity.</p>
<p>It is also important to note that the lower down the focus in the conduction system, and therefore the more prolonged the QRS, the less reliable and the less stable it is.</p>
<p>The above method for calculating the heart rate does not work for rates that fall below 40 bpm. Another way to calculate the heart rate is by following the steps below:</p>
<ol>
<li>Count the 3-second intervals, which you will find at the bottom of every EKG strip.</li>
<li>Count the number of cycles within these intervals (which will amount to 6 seconds in total).</li>
<li>Then multiply this by 10 (10 x 6 seconds = 1 minute) to accurately calculate the beats per minute.</li>
</ol>
<p>An impulse that is generated from below the sinus node (i.e., impulses that are initiated from the</p>
<p>AV node and the ventricles) is called a junctional rhythm. The absence of the P wave is representative of a junctional rhythm. Junctional rhythms are often related to overmedication. The nurse should, therefore, always examine the patient's medications whenever they note the junctional rhythm on EKG. The following medications necessitate close examination in the presence of junctional rhythms: antiarrhythmic medications, beta blockers, calcium channel blockers, and digoxin.</p>
<p>Before learning how to evaluate the rhythm, it is important to recap and remember the following fundamentals:</p>
<ul>
<li>The normal heart rate is 60 – 100 bpm.</li>
<li>A slow heart rate below 60 bpm is referred to as bradycardia.</li>
<li>A fast heart rate above 100 bpm is referred to as tachycardia.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-11-arrhythmias-in-the-ecg">Chapter 11. Arrhythmias in the ECG<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-11-arrhythmias-in-the-ecg" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="normal-sinus-rhythm">Normal Sinus Rhythm<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#normal-sinus-rhythm" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Before we start to analyze any ECG we need to establish what a basic rhythm looks like. The basic rhythm is a normal sinus rhythm, which consists of a P-wave, QRS complex, and a T</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-6a64c9ae.jpeg" alt="img-64.jpeg"></p>
<p>The T wave represents ventricular re-polarization or getting the cells ready for another depolarization.</p>
<p>The PR interval is the time interval between the P wave and the onset of the QRS, which reflects the time between sino-atrial (SA) node and ventricular depolarization. The normal duration is between 3 and 5 small squares (0.12 - 0.2 seconds).</p>
<p>The SA node is responsible for (in normal sinus rhythm) atrial depolarization. The AV node (in normal sinus rhythm) initiates ventricular depolarization.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="sinus-bradycardia">Sinus Bradycardia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sinus-bradycardia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This rhythm is the same as normal sinus rhythm, however the rate is less than 60 beats per minute.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-659077b5.jpeg" alt="img-65.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="sinus-tachycardia">Sinus Tachycardia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sinus-tachycardia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-d551ae48.jpeg" alt="img-66.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="atrial-fibrillation">Atrial Fibrillation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#atrial-fibrillation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Atrial fibrillation simply refers to the atria fibrillating or quivering. Atrial fibrillation is characterized by a lack of definitive P waves. The QRS complexes and T waves will be present, however, the rate of the rhythm will be irregular, generally somewhere between 120 to 160 beats per minute. It is, however, possible for atrial fibrillation to be slower than 120 beats per minute,</p>
<p>however, it is less common. Slow atrial fibrillation can range from rates between 50 and 120 beats per minute.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-92ba180f.jpeg" alt="img-67.jpeg"></p>
<p>So, to follow the method I determine that there are no definitive P waves, there are QRS complexes and T waves; the rhythm is irregular, and it is irregularly irregular.</p>
<p>Actual fibrillation is a phenomenon where an overexcited area of the atria is firing at anywhere around 300 beats per minute. Although the atria are firing at this rate, the AV node will not allow all the impulses to depolarize the ventricles. The atrial activity does not look like normal P waves as the electrical impulse is originating from somewhere within the atria and not following the normal conduction pathway.</p>
<p>The normal conduction pathway originates from the SA node to the AV node, down the Bundle of His, bundle branches, and through to the Purkinje fibers. The normal conduction pathway consists of myelinated fibers which transmit impulses at incredibly fast speeds. This is displayed by narrow complexes, for example, the QRS complex, within 3 small squares or 0.12 seconds. If an impulse originates outside of the normal conduction pathway, the complexes would be broad and bizarre in appearance. In atrial fibrillation, the atrial activity is so fast that it may not be seen on the ECG, and if it is seen, they resemble nothing like the normal P waves. In fact, these atrial depolarizations are generally referred to as f waves.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ventricular-fibrillation">Ventricular Fibrillation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ventricular-fibrillation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Ventricular fibrillation, like atrial fibrillation, refers to the ventricles quivering. A hugely significant difference between atrial fibrillation and ventricular fibrillation is that ventricular fibrillation is a life-threatening rhythm that needs emergency treatment in the form of electrical cardioversion, cardiopulmonary resuscitation, and advanced life support.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-2af7959a.jpeg" alt="img-68.jpeg"></p>
<p>Ventricular fibrillation is shown as an unsystematic wiggling, jagged, spiky appearance. The rhythm should be confirmed by checking the ECG leads are attached properly to the patient. Giving the patient a brief but definite shake at this point would also not be frowned upon! They should be no P-wave, QRS complexes that are unrecognizable from how it should appear and no definitive T wave.</p>
<p>The reason ventricular fibrillation is a life-threatening rhythm is that the ventricles are the main pumping chambers within the heart, and if they are made ineffective, there will be no circulating volume to the rest of the body, and subsequently, death will follow if not rapidly treated. With atrial fibrillation, the atria only supply  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>10</mn><mo>−</mo><mn>30</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">%</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10 - 30\%</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">10</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">−</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8056em; vertical-align: -0.0556em;"></span><span class="mord">30%</span></span></span></span></span>  of the cardiac output, so if the atria are not pumping as normal, the body can overcome it, however, if the ventricles aren't pumping properly, nothing can overcome that besides emergency lifesaving intervention.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="atrial-flutter">Atrial Flutter<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#atrial-flutter" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Atrial flutter is a rhythm that has definite P waves (which in atrial flutter are actually referred to as F waves), which are saw-tooth in appearance. There are QRS complexes and T waves. The ventricular rhythm may be regular or irregular.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-4dac8786.jpeg" alt="img-69.jpeg"></p>
<p>Intrinsically with atrial flutter, again, there is overexcitation of the atria. However, differing from atrial fibrillation, atrial flutter is where the atria are depolarizing continuously and regularly, which gives the saw-tooth appearance.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="atrial-or-supraventricular-tachycardia">Atrial or Supraventricular Tachycardia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#atrial-or-supraventricular-tachycardia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A supra-ventricular tachycardia (SVT) is basically any tachycardia that originates from above the ventricles (hence supra), so in theory, sinus tachycardia is an SVT as the rhythm is originating from the SA node. An SVT will usually have a narrow ( <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo>≤</mo><mn>0.12</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\leq 0.12</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7719em; vertical-align: -0.136em;"></span><span class="mrel">≤</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">0.12</span></span></span></span></span>  seconds or 3 small squares) QRS complex unless the patient has a bundle branch block or aberrant conduction. If an overexcited area of the atria is firing and then the impulse travels down the myelinated fibers; the impulse then passes through the AV node, and the ventricles depolarize at a rapid rate. Because the impulse is traveling down the myelinated pathways and through the AV node, the impulse is traveling very quickly, so the QRS complexes are narrow.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-97e35cb3.jpeg" alt="img-70.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ventricular-tachycardia">Ventricular Tachycardia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ventricular-tachycardia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Ventricular tachycardia, in theory, should have a P wave in it, however, in practice, this is hard to see. The QRS complex will be uniformly spiky or uniformly rounded in appearance and will have a definite shape that repeats itself, which is very unlike ventricular fibrillation. If an ECG is taken, there should be broad ventricular complexes  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex-error" title="ParseError: KaTeX parse error: Expected 'EOF', got '&amp;' at position 1: &amp;̲gt;0.14" style="color: rgb(204, 0, 0);">&amp;gt;0.14</span></span>  seconds, with upright QRS in V1 and negative in V6, in addition, there should be an extreme left axis deviation. There is a T wave, but again, this may not be seen. The treatment of this will depend upon whether the patient has a pulse or not.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="extrasystoles">Extrasystoles<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#extrasystoles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="atrial-ectopics-supra-ventricular-extrasystole">Atrial Ectopics / Supra-Ventricular Extrasystole<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#atrial-ectopics-supra-ventricular-extrasystole" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Atrial ectopics are early beats that originate from the atria. Basically, what you will see is a P wave that is not exactly the same shape as the other P waves, that comes earlier in the rhythm than expected, followed by a QRS, T wave, and then a compensatory pause. Because they originate from within the atria and not from the SA node, they will take longer to depolarize the atria, and as such, the shape will be broader than normal - although it may not be a noticeable widening. In the following example, the P wave is actually hidden in the R wave (the first initial upward deflection in the QRS).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-6bec4172.jpeg" alt="img-71.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ventricular-ectopics-ventricular-extrasystole">Ventricular Ectopics / Ventricular Extrasystole<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ventricular-ectopics-ventricular-extrasystole" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Ventricular ectopics are simply early ventricular beats that do not originate from the AV node as such, the depolarization takes longer, and the QRS is, therefore, wider (&gt;0.12 seconds or 3 small squares) and more bizarre in its morphology. The patient may or may not have a pulse with the ventricular ectopic. In the following example, if this ectopic or extrasystole occurs again and is the same shape, it would be called mono or uni-focal in origin.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-0573643b.jpeg" alt="img-72.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="bav-atrioventricular-block">BAV: Atrioventricular Block<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bav-atrioventricular-block" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="i-degree">I Degree<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-degree" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>First degree heart block is very simply a lengthened PR interval greater than 5 small squares or 0.2 seconds. This rhythm is often a sign of a diseased AV node, however, it is also seen in myocarditis, electrolyte disturbances, and sometimes following certain drugs that increase the refractory time of the AV node (including, but not limited to calcium channel blockers and beta blockers). This rhythm is benign, however people in a first degree heart block are more likely to develop into a second degree heart block if they are given certain anti-arrhythmic medications. In the following example the first two lines show the most obvious first degree heart block.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-5b7fea8a.jpeg" alt="img-73.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ii-degree">II Degree<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ii-degree" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="second-degree-heart-block-mobitz-type-1-wenckebach">Second degree heart block Mobitz type 1 / Wenckebach<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#second-degree-heart-block-mobitz-type-1-wenckebach" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Second degree heart block (type 1) is also known as Wenckebach, after its founder Karel Frederik Wenckebach in 1899. It is nearly always due to a diseased AV node. This rhythm is basically a progressive lengthening of the PR interval until there is a P wave with no QRS depolarization followed by another P wave, a QRS complex and a T wave. The PR intervals get longer and longer until the QRS is dropped. In the following example there is a 4:1 block (4 conducted QRS beats and 1 dropped QRS beat as shown with the red circles).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-45b19d60.jpeg" alt="img-74.jpeg"></p>
<p>If you ever see a P wave not followed by a QRS depolarization and another P wave, then you have a second degree heart block at the very least.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="second-degree-heart-block-mobitz-type-2">Second degree heart block Mobitz type 2<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#second-degree-heart-block-mobitz-type-2" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>With second degree heart block (type 2) there is often a normal intrinsic beat with P, QRS and T waves, followed by a P wave, no QRS and then another intrinsic beat. If this pattern continues it would be classed as a second degree heart block (type 2) with a 1:1 block. The 1:1 block refers to 1 normal beat : 1 dropped QRS.</p>
<p>It is however more common to see a second degree heart block (type 2) with a 2:1 (as shown below), or a 3:1 heart block. This is basically two or three normal intrinsic beats, followed by a P wave with no QRS. It then continues with the same pattern.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-6ae07c14.jpeg" alt="img-75.jpeg"></p>
<p>It is also possible and not too uncommon to have a second degree heart block (type 2) with a variable block. So, in this instance, you may have one intrinsic beat with 1 dropped QRS, followed by 3 intrinsic beats and a dropped QRS, next followed by 2 intrinsic beats, and so on.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="iii-grade">III Grade<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#iii-grade" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This rhythm is basically a disassociation between the atria and the ventricles, where the atria will depolarize at about 60-70 beats per minute, and the ventricles will beat at about 40 beats per minute.</p>
<p>Both the atria (P waves) and the ventricles (QRS complexes) are regular in their respective rates, but they are not associated with one another; for example, atria at 60 beats per minute and ventricles at 40 beats per minute.</p>
<p>The following example shows the ventricular rate at approximately 30 beats per minute and the atria at approximately 75 beats per minute. Note the ventricular rate and atrial rate are different.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-4c4f1d4f.jpeg" alt="img-76.jpeg"></p>
<p>Often patients with this rhythm are unstable and need urgent cardiac pacing.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="left-and-right-branch-block">Left and Right Branch Block<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#left-and-right-branch-block" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Below is an image of the heart with the associated conduction structures:</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-fcc07420.jpeg" alt="img-77.jpeg"></p>
<ol>
<li>SA node</li>
<li>AV node</li>
<li>Bundle of His</li>
<li>Left bundle branch</li>
<li>Left posterior fascicle</li>
<li>Left anterior fascicle</li>
<li>Left ventricle</li>
<li>Ventricular septum</li>
<li>Right ventricle</li>
<li>Right bundle branch</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="left-bundle-branch-block">Left bundle branch block<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#left-bundle-branch-block" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This morphology happens because the left bundle branch is unable to conduct impulses. Bundle branch blocks are easy to remember. If you can remember the words "william" and "marrow," you will be halfway there. Very basically, with a left bundle branch block, there is a "W" shape</p>
<p>in V1 and an "M" shape in V6 - hence "William". The morphology does not exactly follow an M or W shape but comes close. To be more precise, there is a QS or rS in V1 and a RsR in V6. The non-capital letters reflect a small wave, and the capital letters refer to large waves. Bundle branch blocks must have a QRS which is greater than 3 small squares or 0.12 seconds. Left bundle branch blocks are serious: if they are new, then it is very possible that the patient is having a myocardial infarction. However, it may also be due to aortic stenosis and dilated cardiomyopathies. The following example shows a left bundle branch block with atrial ectopics. Some authors believe that if you have a left bundle branch block you therefore have a bifascicular block.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="right-bundle-branch-block">Right bundle branch block<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#right-bundle-branch-block" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>With the right bundle branch block there is a "M" shape in V1 and a "W" shape in V6. To be more precise in the morphology of the right bundle branch block an rSR is seen in V1 and qRS in V6. Remember that the QRS must be greater than 0.12 seconds or 3 small squares.</p>
<p>Right bundle branch blocks are benign in their ECG alone.</p>
<p>A right bundle branch block is often present when increased right sided heart pressures are present, for example with a pulmonary embolism, hypertension, cardiomyopathies, congenital heart disease and trauma to the right ventricle.</p>
<p>Again, the M and W shape are not exact but come close.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pea-pulseless-electrical-activity">PEA: Pulseless Electrical Activity<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pea-pulseless-electrical-activity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Electro-Mechanical-Dissociation / Pulseless Electrical Activity is another life-threatening rhythm where cardiopulmonary resuscitation and advanced life support is needed. It is shown by any life sustaining rhythm, however there is no pulse associated with it. An example of this would be sinus bradycardia on the monitor accompanied by a lack of pulse.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-fb3668bd.jpeg" alt="img-78.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="asystole">Asystole<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#asystole" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Asystole is shown as no cardiac activity. No P, QRS or T waves. The trace is often undulating in appearance and not truly a flat line. This trace needs to be confirmed in 2 ECG leads and the amplitude, or ECG gain, should be turned right up to exclude fine ventricular fibrillation. This is another life-threatening arrhythmia that requires urgent cardiopulmonary resuscitation and advanced life support.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-8e11ef9b.jpeg" alt="img-79.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="sca-stemi-nstemi">SCA / STEMI / NSTEMI<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sca-stemi-nstemi" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The earliest sign of a myocardial infarction is a hyperacute T-wave followed by ST-segment elevation, T-wave inversion, and Q-wave formation.</p>
<p>The ST-segment returns to baseline. T-wave inversion persists and may deepen while Q-waves remain. The T-wave becomes upright eventually. This is also known as STEMI (ST-elevation MI)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-c52e2ebc.jpeg" alt="img-80.jpeg"></p>
<p>Chest pain with elevated ST segments in two anatomically contiguous (adjacent) leads on ECG and elevated serum troponin confirms the diagnosis of myocardial infarction. However, a normal</p>
<p>appearing ECG and normal serum troponin do not mean the patient is healthy when in reality patient history and symptoms suggest myocardial injury. In very early stages of infarction, the initial ECG and serum troponin may be normal. In such instances, serial ECGs and troponin levels will be necessary to confirm the diagnosis.</p>
<p>Be aware, certain patient populations present with atypical symptoms.</p>
<ul>
<li>Atypical presentation may present with non-chest pain symptoms, such as weakness, nausea, sweating, cough, dyspnea, pain in the back, jaw, or head.</li>
<li>Females, elderly individuals, and those with diabetes often present with atypical symptoms.</li>
<li>Keep your index of suspicion for ischemia and/or myocardial infarction high when evaluating such patients and err on the side of caution when deciding whether to perform a 12-lead ECG.</li>
</ul>
<p>NSTEMI (non-ST-elevation MI) presents as ST-depression and T-wave inversion. No progression to Q-wave exists so this can also be called a non-Q-wave myocardial infarction. Diagnosis is made based on history and clinical markers. This situation may pose a risk of re-infarction.</p>
<p>Anterior MI's refer to the sight of injury. Looking at specific leads can narrow the location further. Anterior MI's are identified in leads V2-V4. Anterior septal MI's are seen in leads V1 - V2 because of septal branch occlusions. When the LAD (left anterior descending artery) or Diagonal is involved, changes will be seen in leads V3 - V4. The changes that are seen include ST-segment elevation, deep Q-waves, &amp; loss of R-wave progression (V1 should show small R-waves which then become larger through precordial leads and largest in V4 before becoming small in V6 again). Anterior MI's can lead to heart failure, shock, atrial &amp; ventricular dysrhythmias, Mobitz II (intermittent, non-conducted P-waves without progressive prolongation of PR-interval), complete heart block (CHB), bundle branch block (BBB), ventricular septal defect (VSD), rupture, or aneurysm.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pacemaker">Pacemaker<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pacemaker" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Wandering atrial pacemaker (WAP), like multifocal atrial tachycardia, is caused by frequent and unpredictable beats in the atria that are discharged from different areas (foci) within the atrium. It is similar to MAT, except the heart rate is usually slower (less than 100 bpm).</p>
<p>EKG characteristics of Wandering Atrial Pacemaker:</p>
<p>An irregular rhythm that is caused by impulses being triggered from different sites.
Normal heart rate: 60 - 100 bpm.
At least three different types of P waves
P-QRS ratio: 1:1.</p>
<ul>
<li>Variations in the PR intervals.
Normal QRS complex forms: 0.06 - 0.10 seconds.</li>
</ul>
<p>Like MAT, the wandering atrial pacemaker is usually asymptomatic and doesn't usually necessitate treatment.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="torsade-de-pointes">Torsade de Pointes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#torsade-de-pointes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Torsade de Pointes or Polymorphic ventricular tachycardia looks the same a ventricular tachycardia, however the amplitude of the QRS undulates. This is due to a rotation of the electrical axis or direction of electrical impulses.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-12-exercises-in-ecg-interpretation">Chapter 12. Exercises in ECG Interpretation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-12-exercises-in-ecg-interpretation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-8b861ddf.jpeg" alt="img-81.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="1-what-is-normal-sinus-rhythm-nsr">1. What is Normal Sinus Rhythm (NSR)?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-what-is-normal-sinus-rhythm-nsr" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Normal sinus rhythm (NSR) is an impulse that originates in the sinus node. It is recognized by the P wave morphology:</p>
<p>Upright P in leads I, II and aVF
inverted in aVR
P wave is variable in leads III and aVL
P wave is upright in leads V4-V6
Most often biphasic (positive-negative) in leads V1 and V2</p>
<ul>
<li>Minor variations in P wave morphology related to respiratory cycle</li>
</ul>
<p>PR interval (PRI) exceeds 120 ms or 0.12 s and can vary slightly with rate.</p>
<p>Rate can be between 60-100 beats/min. Most cardiologists agree that the operational limits for NSR range from 50-90 beats/min.</p>
<p>Rates lower than 60 beats/min are bradycardia and rates higher than 100 beats/min are tachycardia.</p>
<p>During some AV blocks, the P waves are not followed by a QRS. You still interpret it as sinus rhythm (bradycardia or tachycardia). So, you will see interpretations like sinus rhythm, complete heart block with idioventricular escape.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-62d268e7.jpeg" alt="img-82.jpeg">
2. Computing the rate: the Big square and small square method</p>
<p>The quickest method to estimate the rate is to use the BIG SQUARE METHOD (shown in the image). The R to R is 8 big squares (less than 50). Another way to is to use the SMALL SQUARE METHOD (Rate = 1500/ #small squares or in this case  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>1500</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><mn>40</mn><mo>=</mo><mn>37</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">1500 / 40 = 37</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mord">1500/40</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">37</span></span></span></span></span>  beats/min). P waves are upright in I, II and aVF and inverted in aVR, then this is sinus bradycardia.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-c83be340.jpeg" alt="img-83.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="3-what-is-the-interpretation">3. What is the interpretation?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-what-is-the-interpretation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-5c9deba6.jpeg" alt="img-84.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="middle-of-the-wenckebach">Middle of the Wenckebach<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#middle-of-the-wenckebach" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There are 3 possible interpretations for this strip.</p>
<ol>
<li>Sinus tachycardia (ST) with long PRI (first degree AV block). The atrial and ventricular rate is about 100 bpm with a prolonged PR interval (PRI) of 0.44 sec. It is possible to see P waves between a QRS and T wave (as in this case).</li>
<li>Sinus tachycardia with accelerated junctional rhythm (creating AV dissociation). The ventricles can be captured by an impulse coming from the AV junction and not the sinoatrial node (SAN). The atrium and the ventricle are beating independent of each other or is DISSOCIATED. The inverted P from the junctional beat cannot be seen because the impulse from the junction going up to the atrium is blocked.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="3-middle-of-the-wenckebach-cycle">3. Middle of the Wenckebach cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-middle-of-the-wenckebach-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The strip below is from the same patient showing the end and beginning of the Wenckebach cycle. ECG # 3 is the middle part of this whole cycle. Remember that the heart is continuously beating. The ECG is like a photograph or a snapshot of cardiac electrical activity.</p>
<p>You may be correct if the strip was interpreted as the above 2 but in this clinical scenario, ECG # 3 is part of the Wenckebach. So, there can be more than one possible interpretation of a strip.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-e9ae826e.jpeg" alt="img-85.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="4-what-is-the-ecg-interpretation">4. What is the ECG interpretation?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-what-is-the-ecg-interpretation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-dda144ec.jpeg" alt="img-86.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="second-degree-av-block-type-i-or-mobitz-i-wenckebach">Second Degree AV Block Type I or Mobitz I (Wenckebach)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#second-degree-av-block-type-i-or-mobitz-i-wenckebach" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is sinus rhythm with 3 P waves (red arrows) for every 2 QRS (3:2). There is prolongation of the second PRI before the non-conducted P wave. This is typical Wenckebach pattern. A quick way to spot Wenckebach during sinus rhythm is to compare the PRI of the last conducted beat (blue asterisk) which is 0.24 sec or 6 small squares with the PRI of the first conducted beat (red asterisk) which is 0.20 sec or 5 small squares. The PRI of the first conducted beat is shorter compared to the PRI of the last conducted beat. The obvious prolongation of the PRI in the second conducted beat is another quick way.</p>
<p>As you master the art and the more strips you see, "group-beating" of the QRS is (in the words of</p>
<p>Dr. Marriott) "footprints of a Wenckebach". So, when you see "group-beating" suspect a Wenckebach.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-5a6ec084.jpeg" alt="img-87.jpeg">
5. What is the ECG interpretation?</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-78204f5a.jpeg" alt="img-88.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="second-degree-av-block-type-ii-or-mobitz-ii">Second degree AV block type II or Mobitz II<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#second-degree-av-block-type-ii-or-mobitz-ii" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The strip showed dropped beats with 3:2 and 2:1 AV conduction. The PR interval before the dropped beat did not increase (but prolonged at 0.24 sec or  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>240</mn><mtext>&nbsp;</mtext><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">240~\mathrm{ms}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">240</span><span class="mspace nobreak">&nbsp;</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">ms</span></span></span></span></span></span> ). Permanent pacemaker implantation was done. You do not see this all the time. This is rare.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-4f708eca.jpeg" alt="img-89.jpeg">
6. What kind of heart block?</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-35be96cb.jpeg" alt="img-90.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="advanced-heart-block-or-high-grade-av-block">Advanced heart block or high-grade AV block<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#advanced-heart-block-or-high-grade-av-block" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When 2 or more P waves are non-conducted in a row, the term high-grade or advanced second degree AV block is applied.</p>
<p>Reference: Goldberger A. 2013. Goldberger’s Clinical Electrocardiography : A Simplified Approach. 8Ed Ph Elsevier</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-aec88e60.jpeg" alt="img-91.jpeg">

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="atrial-fibrillation-with-complete-heart-block">Atrial fibrillation with complete heart block<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#atrial-fibrillation-with-complete-heart-block" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The ECG shows regular wide QRS rhythm (RBBB morphology) at  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo>∼</mo><mn>30</mn></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\sim 30</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.3669em;"></span><span class="mrel">∼</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">30</span></span></span></span></span>  bpm with no discernible P waves. There are no flutter waves to explain the regular ventricular response. This ventricular rate is too slow for a junctional rhythm (which may conduct with RBBB morphology). The rhythm that can explain this is atrial fibrillation with complete heart block with idioventricular rhythm. A pacemaker was eventually placed.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-567d286f.jpeg" alt="img-93.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="8-what-is-the-cause-of-the-sudden-bradycardia">8. What is the cause of the sudden bradycardia?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#8-what-is-the-cause-of-the-sudden-bradycardia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260119-5c9ec0a1.jpeg" alt="img-94.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="non-conducted-premature-atrial-beat-in-bigeminy">Non-conducted premature atrial beat in bigeminy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#non-conducted-premature-atrial-beat-in-bigeminy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Use all leads to look for the "missing". The best candidate is V lead. The T waves in QRS # 1 and 2 are not as pointed compared to the next T waves. So, this are P waves "sitting" on the T waves. This P waves are too early for the sinus cycle. So, the cause of the bradycardia is non-conducted PAB in bigeminy. It is also noticeable that the some of the QRS (red arrowhead) are conducted with aberration.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="atrial-flutter-with-2-1-av-conduction">Atrial flutter with 2:1 AV conduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#atrial-flutter-with-2-1-av-conduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This is a regular narrow complex tachycardia at a rate of about 150 bpm. ECG machine often misinterpret these tracing because of the flutter waves distorting ST segments. This is actually atrial flutter with 2:1 AV conduction simulating an inferior wall STEMI. The 12L as slower ventricular response clearly show those flutter waves.</p>
