<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="paroxysmal-supraventricular-tachycardia-svt-with-aberrancy-with-spontaneous-normalization">Paroxysmal supraventricular tachycardia (SVT) with aberrancy with spontaneous normalization<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#paroxysmal-supraventricular-tachycardia-svt-with-aberrancy-with-spontaneous-normalization" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The middle of the strip shows a regular tachycardia that started with a PAC (blue arrowhead). The next 9 beats had a right bundle branch block or RBBB configuration (circled black) and later the QRS width normalized (black line). There are (inverted) P waves (red arrowhead) right after the QRS in II, III and aVF as well as in the V lead. This is paroxysmal supraventricular tachycardia (SVT) with aberrancy with spontaneous normalization. This type of SVT can be described as a short RP tachycardia.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-13-hypertrophy-and-enlargement">Chapter 13: Hypertrophy And Enlargement<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-13-hypertrophy-and-enlargement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The terms "cardiac hypertrophy" and "cardiac enlargement" can be confusing, and you might be tempted to think they are the same thing when they really aren't. The word "hypertrophy" as applied to the heart means "muscle thickening." You need to see more muscle in the heart to be able to use this term. The ventricles are almost always the source of thickening. There just isn't enough muscle in atrial walls to have the thickening mean much.</p>
<p>Cardiac enlargement, on the other hand, means the heart is bigger. The atria can easily enlarge, especially in atrial fibrillation, when the atria just fill with blood and never contract. The ventricles, too, can simply enlarge without being hypertrophic (thickened). On an x-ray, you will only see a big heart silhouette on the film, showing heart enlargement but not indicating if the muscle is thick or not. You'll need an ECG or other imaging system to tell the difference.</p>
<p>Diseases of the heart muscle that cause hypertrophy or enlargement of the heart are called cardiomyopathies. Not all cardiomyopathies are the same, and the causes are many.</p>
<p>You can have cardiomyopathy from valvular disease, coronary artery disease, and hereditary or congenital disorders.</p>
<p>Most of them fit neatly into three main categories: dilated cardiomyopathy, restrictive cardiomyopathy, and hypertrophic cardiomyopathy. Let's look at what to expect from these different subtypes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="the-p-wave-and-atrial-enlargement">The P Wave and Atrial Enlargement<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-p-wave-and-atrial-enlargement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The P wave is the pacing signal that begins the depolarization wave, which begins the heartbeat by starting the heart muscle to contract. If you have a heart pacemaker, it puts out a P wave "on-demand," and you never see it. If your heart happens to have a P wave that begins a contraction before it is supposed to, you will see that P wave called an ectopic P wave. The source of arrhythmia is a P wave that becomes a normal part of a heartbeat. There are two types of ectopic P waves. The obvious one is when you get the PVT, where there is no P wave to begin the depolarization wave, which is never a good thing. Your next arrhythmia might be called fibrillation. If you suddenly get an ectopic P wave, you should know it means the heart is fibrillating (fibrillating means "fibrillation"). This term is technically incorrect because P waves still occur in fibrillation. Now, some P waves can be due to mechanical problems in the heart, but most of the time it means some type of heart block is happening, and your heart is incapable of generating a normal heartbeat.</p>
<p>Atrial enlargement can be from a lot of things that have nothing to do with the heart muscle being thickened, but it does have everything to do with the size of the atria.</p>
<p>Atrial enlargement of the left ventricle comes from excessive filling of this chamber. It was designed to handle a load of blood; however, if a certain limit is reached, the atrium can't contract, and fibrillation occurs as the chamber turns into a big bag of blood that just sits there. It does have some ability to hypertrophy, though, called LAH or left atrial hypertrophy.</p>
<p>As you can imagine, the P wave will be abnormal on the ECG to some degree. Remember that hypertrophy translates into a bigger deflection on the ECG. If you see a bigger P wave "bump" on the first part of the P wave in lead II, it's right atrial hypertrophy, while if it's bigger on the other side, it's left. You might see two big bumps if both atria have hypertrophy.</p>
<p>V1 normally has a biphasic P wave so that you can look at the "bump size" on this lead as well. Don't forget that, when you look at an ECG, the left side of the P wave (the first half) represents the right atrium, while the right side of the P wave (the second half) is actually the left side of the patient (and the left atrium).</p>
<p>The reason you might see LAH by itself is if the person has mitral stenosis. A narrowed mitral valve is rough on the left atrium because it cannot push the blood out fast enough as it contracts, so some don't make it into the left ventricle with each beat. Over time, the atrium will hypertrophy to try and overcome the blockage. If this doesn't work, the next step is atrial fibrillation.</p>
<p>On the other hand, left atrial hypertrophy can also be seen along with left ventricular hypertrophy. In severe systemic hypertension, aortic stenosis, hypertrophic cardiomyopathy, or mitral regurgitation, you might see both LVH and LAH together. In this case, the mitral valve is leaky and not blocked, but the effect is the same. In both cases, the pressure inside the left atrium is just too great, so the chamber walls thicken in response to this.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="right-atrial-enlargement">Right Atrial Enlargement<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#right-atrial-enlargement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The right atrium is very responsive to atrial enlargement, which causes it to enlarge more than is normal. Right atrial enlargement is called "SVT" for short because the P wave so often produces the same kind of ventricular tachycardia you see on the ECG. The P wave is made the same way as the P wave on the left is, except it goes through the right atrium instead of the left side. This is because the right side of the heart is always contracting and normally has a different electrical cycle than the left side. The only real difference is that the right side of the heart is less responsive to activation, which means that the P wave is more of a constant electrical depolarization signal, and it's a little easier to make a big P wave on the right side.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="left-atrial-enlargement">Left Atrial Enlargement<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#left-atrial-enlargement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Left atrial enlargement is usually more common than right. If the atrium is so dilated that it is unable to contract effectively, you have atrial fibrillation. It is one of the main causes of the infamous SVT, so you might see it in people who have frequent heart palpitations, or people who have sustained fibrillation. Left atrial enlargement is due to several problems.</p>
<p>One of the most common causes is hyperthyroidism, where the body is just constantly overactive. This means it's bouncing the heart muscle too fast, and the atrium can't really contract enough to prevent a P wave from being a big, scary one.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="right-ventricular-hypertrophy">Right Ventricular Hypertrophy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#right-ventricular-hypertrophy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is the enlargement of an organ due to an increase in the size of cells. The right ventricular hypertrophy, therefore, is an enlargement of the right ventricle.</p>
<p>This may be caused by many different conditions, including:</p>
<ul>
<li>Pulmonary hypertension</li>
<li>Mitral stenosis</li>
<li>Pulmonary embolism</li>
<li>Chronic lung disease</li>
<li>Arrhythmogenic right ventricular cardiomyopathy</li>
</ul>
<p>Congenital heart diseases</p>
<p>To identify right ventricular hypertrophy on an ECG, look for:</p>
<p>Axis: RAD for the patient's age.</p>
<p>Voltages: R waves are tall in right leads. S waves are deep in left-sided leads.</p>
<p>R/S ratio: The R/S ratio is abnormal. It is increased in V1-2 and is less than one in V6 after the age of one month.</p>
<p>T waves: T waves are upright in V1 and V4R up to six years of age when they are normal elsewhere. This implies significant RVH.</p>
<p>Q waves: There is a qR pattern in V1, meaning there is a small Q wave to a tall R wave.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="left-ventricular-hypertrophy">Left Ventricular Hypertrophy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#left-ventricular-hypertrophy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>It is the enlargement of the left ventricle. This occurs most commonly due to hypertension but may be caused by other conditions, such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>Aortic stenosis</li>
<li>Aortic regurgitation</li>
<li>Mitral regurgitation</li>
<li>Hypertrophic cardiomyopathy</li>
<li>Coarctation of the aorta</li>
</ul>
<p>To identify this on an ECG, look for:</p>
<p>Axis: LAD for the patient's age.</p>
<p>Voltages: There will be tall R waves on left leads and deep S waves on the right-side leads.</p>
<p>R/S ratio: There will be an abnormal R/S ratio, favoring the LV. There will be a decreased ratio in V1 and 2.</p>
<p>Q waves: Q waves will be abnormal in V5 and V6.</p>
<p>Inverted T waves: Present in I and aVL.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="repolarization-abnormalities">Repolarization Abnormalities<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#repolarization-abnormalities" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In normal hearts, you should be able to see a constant P wave, with no discernible T waves. In diabetics, you see a couple of things that can alter the appearance of the P wave.</p>
<p>The first is P waves with an S-T pattern. This means the P wave is very tall (eqn. PR + ST-segment = pattern seen) in the early part of the R wave, then the P wave becomes flattened (longer in the taller pattern), and finally is very short (later in the shorter pattern).</p>
<p>The P wave pattern with the ST pattern is normal.</p>
<p>The second is that when people are in atrial fibrillation when the chambers are fibrillating, the chambers are making very fast beats, so fast they are not even visible. Since the heart can't be still when it's fibrillating, the chambers appear to have very fast, shallow beats.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="echocardiography">Echocardiography<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#echocardiography" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Echocardiography, also called echocardiography, is a technique by which sound waves are sent through the patient's heart, through the chest, through the flesh, and back into a probe that was</p>
<p>placed on the chest. You then get visual images on a screen of the chambers of the heart that are enlarged, have hypertrophy, have atrial fibrillation, have an abnormal amount of blood sloshing around. You can also see pulmonary heart disease this way because the lungs are filled with blood flow. Physical examination, the ECG, and an electrocardiogram can all give you great information, but it is good to supplement this with echocardiography, because the patient's response to this examination is very non-specific, meaning the patient may not feel bad but might have an abnormal heart.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="chapter-14-miscellaneous-conditions">Chapter 14: Miscellaneous Conditions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chapter-14-miscellaneous-conditions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-effect-of-drugs-on-conduction">The Effect of Drugs on Conduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-effect-of-drugs-on-conduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Certain drugs can slow the heart rate and prolonged use might, therefore, cause prolonged QRS and occasionally bundle branch block. They can also impair conduction which might show up on ECG as wide QRS (e.g., quinine) or prolonged QRS (e.g., digitalis, disopyramide, procainamide). Drugs that inhibit adrenergic activity, like antihypertensives or B-blockers, can also cause a prolonged QTc interval. Moreover, drugs may prolong repolarization times due to Arrhythmogenic Effect.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-athlete-s-heart">The Athlete's Heart<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-athlete-s-heart" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Exercise strengthens the heart muscle and, hence, normalizes any physiological conduction abnormalities. The QRS complex, which normally ranges from eighty to one hundred ten milliseconds, may become even shorter during exercise, mainly due to enhanced pacemaker activity in the sinoatrial node. The bundle branches are also affected by exercise.</p>
<p>The left bundle shows increased isochronal premature depolarizations during exercise. Also, the right bundle is larger in athletes than in non-athletic subjects. The overall physiologic effect of exercise on the ECG is largely benign. There is only a subtle decrease in the left and right bundle signals and a slight increase in the T-wave amplitude. However, in an exaggerated and prolonged exercise, the ECG may show other alterations, for example, left ventricular hypertrophy, prominent precordial T-waves, mild heart block, and other minimal abnormalities.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="preparticipation-screening-for-athletes">Preparticipation Screening for Athletes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#preparticipation-screening-for-athletes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Preparticipation screening is being used in the United States in an effort to identify individuals who are at risk of cardiovascular disease because of their sporting activities. For this purpose, athletes are examined in special laboratories for their ECG/EKG to detect any cardiac abnormalities. If significant abnormalities are detected, the athletes are referred to specialized centers for an individualized cardiac screening, addressing the most important cardiovascular diseases. During the routine screening, the ECG shows changes in the athlete's heart. The resting heart rate is typically less than sixty beats per minute.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="sleep-disorders">Sleep Disorders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sleep-disorders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Sleep disorders have been associated with an increased risk of premature cardiovascular death. It has been estimated that from five to nine percent of sudden cardiac deaths occurring in young people with no significant conduction defects can be attributed to sleep apnea and/or pauses in breathing.</p>
<p>Sleep-disordered breathing has been associated with wide and tall complexes and T-wave inversion, and with a low and low-to-intermediate voltage ST segment. There are also a number of sleep disorders that have been linked to the ECG, such as obstructive sleep apnea syndrome, periodic leg movements of sleep, and narcolepsy.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="conduction-disturbance-caused-by-hyperthermia">Conduction Disturbance Caused by Hyperthermia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#conduction-disturbance-caused-by-hyperthermia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>During heat exposure or exercise, the temperature of the body increases up to forty-three degrees Celsius. The temperature of the heart rises dependent upon the temperature of the body. This causes three major changes in the ECG. At first, conduction of the electrical impulse will slow down, but the PR interval remains unchanged. Then, the QRS complex will widen and, finally, the ST segment and the T-wave will also be prolonged. These last two phenomena may mimic myocardial infarction and need to be differentiated from real myocardial infarction by an expert in emergency medicine.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="electrolyte-abnormalities">Electrolyte Abnormalities<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#electrolyte-abnormalities" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Electrolyte abnormalities such as hypo- and hyperkalemia and hypomagnesemia may affect the conduction properties of the heart muscle. This, in turn, affects the QRS complex, which is prolonged due to delayed repolarization or QT prolongation. Hypokalemia may also cause ST-segment depression or T-wave flattening. The electrocardiograms of the hypokalemic patient show prolonged ST segments and flattened T-waves due to prolonged repolarization time.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="drug-induced-electrolyte-abnormalities">Drug-Induced Electrolyte Abnormalities<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#drug-induced-electrolyte-abnormalities" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Drugs are the most common cause of electrolyte disorders. The isoelectric point of an ion is the concentration at which the net electrical charge of the ion is zero. The isoelectric point for potassium, for example, is three point eight. The degree of ionization depends on the pH and other environmental factors. Most of the drugs used every day by medical personnel and patients cause some mild disturbances in the integrity of the plasma membrane. These disturbances affect the ion distribution in the cell and, hence, the electrical charge. Some of these disturbances can affect the liquid distribution in the tissue and, hence, the serum electrolytes that leave the cell and enter the tissues and organs. This is called the transmural spread and may play a role in electrolyte abnormalities in many diseases.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="antidepressant-drugs">Antidepressant Drugs<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#antidepressant-drugs" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Antidepressant drugs create abnormalities similar, but not identical to those caused by catecholamine excess. Some antidepressant drugs will change the electrocardiogram by the same mechanism but cause an increase in the QRS amplitude (i.e., QRS width) since they prolong the PR interval. These are called augmented suppressors. For other drugs, the QRS is normal, but the T-wave is lowered. These are called inverted or negative suppressors.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="anesthesia">Anesthesia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#anesthesia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Anesthesia may cause conduction disturbances, especially when the heart is exhausted after anesthesia, by slowing down the repolarization phase of the cardiac impulse. This, in turn, causes an increased QTc. After surgery or anesthesia, it is advisable that the patient get up right away, drink plenty of fluids, and take aspirin to diminish any QTc prolongation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="postoperatively">Postoperatively<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#postoperatively" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>After surgery, the QR's interval remains the same, but the QRS amplitude becomes widened. This, in turn, causes ST-T segment changes. These are called the QRS prolongation phenomenon. QRS correlates with QT interval. Therefore, QRS prolongation observed after surgery is primarily because of the QT. Devices are being used worldwide to help diagnose cardiac abnormalities in the ER. These are called ECG Holters. They are typically only used during an actual cardiac pause if any. It has been estimated that they can provide around twenty hours of uninterrupted ECG, and can show most of the aforementioned conditions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="congestive-heart-failure">Congestive Heart Failure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#congestive-heart-failure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The most common form of CHF is due to a condition called left ventricular dysfunction. In this type of CHF, the heart cannot propel blood properly through the body. As a result, blood backs up in the left ventricle, causing a fluid gain in the lungs and swollen ankles. Diabetes is a major risk factor for CHF in people in their forties, and in diabetes patients who have systolic dysfunction. The causes of CHF are many. In people with diabetes or who have hypertension, the heart loses contractile function. This loss of contractile force leads to left ventricular dysfunction. The heart loses contractile function, and the blood backs up in the left ventricle, causing fluid to accumulate in the lungs. Finally, the heart cannot pump blood properly into the body, and blood backs up in the left ventricle, causing a buildup of fluid in the lungs and swelling in the ankles.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="advanced-cardiac-life-support-acls">Advanced Cardiac Life Support (ACLS)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#advanced-cardiac-life-support-acls" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This protocol is used when the regular EKG cannot make the diagnosis. It is a regime that uses a series of specific cardiac clinical scales that the medical personnel use to determine a definitive diagnosis. The specific examinations that medical personnel should perform include an assessment of the rhythm, ST-segment deviation, the QT interval, the Q waves, elevated ST Segs, 12 lead EKG, and consultation from telemetry run.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="bedside-echocardiogram">Bedside Echocardiogram<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bedside-echocardiogram" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In the past ten years, it has been recognized that most EKGs are inconclusive. Some have estimated that around eighty to ninety percent of all EKGs cover a spectrum of disorders from normal to probable diagnosis. A bedside echocardiogram can be a useful adjunct to the standard clinical workup of a patient with chest pain and complaints of shortness of breath. It is a real boon in the management of patients with suspected cardiac disease or chest pain. In fact, many patients with cardiac disease may not have any symptoms but will show changes on their ECG as signs for myocardial infarction. A bedside echocardiogram is a useful diagnostic tool especially in patients who complain of shortness of breath and chest pain. It can help screen for the possibility of aortic dissection, valvar insufficiency, obstructive sleep apnea, or hypertensive cardiomyopathy. In the same way, it can be used to make a diagnosis of clinical deterioration post-surgical procedures especially in cases where the gallbladder is removed. As many as ninety percent of all patients with gallbladder problems may not have any symptoms but will show changes on their ECG as signs for myocardial infarction.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="holter-monitor">Holter Monitor<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#holter-monitor" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>It will be worn for 24 hours. It monitors the number of times that the heart beats per minute, and whether or not any arrhythmias occur. Each time an irregularity occurs, it is stored in memory for later analysis. Holter monitor can work well if used properly, but it may lead to false-positive or false-negative results. False positives would suggest conditions similar to cardiac problems when there are none; false negatives fail to identify cardiac abnormalities when they are present. This is because patients tend to exercise less when they are on the monitor during normal daily activities.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="conclusion">Conclusion<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#conclusion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The medical field is as vast and deep, just like an ocean. There are millions of clinical facts, and figures and NO body can remember all of them. Similarly, in one's clinical career, they will meet several patients, but one can't save them all.</p>
<p>But just by acquiring enough and precise medical knowledge, one can make a significant difference in someone's life. Learning how to interpret ECG is one of those lifesaving skills.</p>
<p>ECG, since its time of invention, has made a substantial change in the field of medicine. It is now taken as one of the baseline investigations for the diagnosis of conditions related to the heart. It is non-invasive and easily accessible. It is one of the essential tests for evaluating the abnormalities associated with cardiac rhythm. Timely detection of myocardial ischemia and myocardial infarction is only made possible due to ECG. Defects in the conduction system, preexcitation, long QT syndromes, atrial anomalies, ventricular hypertrophy, pericarditis, are few of other disorders which can be diagnosed by ECG.</p>
<p>As you have seen, without a regular rate and rhythm, the heart will not be able to efficiently pump and circulate oxygenated blood and other nutrients to the tissues and organs of the body. Hopefully, the critical nature of this topic has been impressed upon the reader.</p>
<p>A comprehensive understanding is crucial, and we hope that you will continue to develop your understanding of electrophysiology by viewing and analyzing as many strips as possible. We encourage you to make it a point to seek out real-life examples within your clinical setting. Repetition is the key to successful understanding. Gather all information at your disposal and trust yourself when you are interpreting an EKG.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="putting-it-all-together">Putting It All Together<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#putting-it-all-together" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Using a systematic approach to interpreting EKG's is important so that vital characteristics are not overlooked. Features that require analysis include heart rate, rhythm, intervals, conduction defects, chamber enlargement, and ST &amp; T-wave changes. To conclude this book, below is a step-by-step guide regarding how to evaluate each of these features.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="components">Components<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#components" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Heart rate can be calculated easily if the rhythm is regular by counting the number of 1 mm squares between two heart beats (RR-interval) and dividing this number into 3000 if the paper speed is 50 mm/s (3000 = 1 minute b/c 60 sec. x 50 = 3000), or into 1500 if the paper speed is 25 mm/s (the common standard). If the heart rate is irregular, the average over a period of time must be determined. It is important to note that 30 large squares or 150 mm represent 3 seconds at 50 mm/s or 6 seconds at 25 mm/s. The heart rate can be evaluated by counting the number of QRS complexes over 30 large squares and multiplying by 20 if at 50 mm/s or 10 if at 25 mm/s. Conventionally, a normal heart rate is 60 - 100 bpm. Clinical context is important because a well-trained athlete, for example, may have a heart rate of 85 bpm, which is considered tachycardia.</p>
<p>Rhythm refers to the pattern that the heart beats and the electrical sequence displayed on EKG.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="approach">Approach<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#approach" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Trust your first impression.</li>
</ol>
<p>Examine the EKG briefly and see what jumps out. Often times, pacer spikes, peak T-wave, or other prominent findings can be very telling. Retain this first impression before methodically narrowing your search. Keep it in mind moving forward.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Explore it step-by-step and in detail.</li>
</ol>
<p>Consider each beat and determine if and how they look different from one another. Use the normal beats and their segments, intervals, etc. as comparison. Do you see PVC’s, bundle branch blocks, or anything abnormal?</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Rate?</li>
</ol>
<p>Tachy/Brady, irregular/regular?</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Rhythm?</li>
</ol>
<p>Irregular/regular, P-waves present and uniform, P-to-QRS ratio, complexes, intervals, wide/narrow, depressed, peaked, notched?</p>
<ol start="5">
<li>Hypertrophy?</li>
</ol>
<p>LAH, RAH, LVH, RVH, strain patterns?</p>
<ol start="6">
<li>Ischemia or Infarct?</li>
</ol>
<p>T-wave &amp; ST-segment abnormalities, Q-waves?</p>
<ol start="7">
<li>List.</li>
</ol>
<p>Record rate, rhythm, hypertrophy, intervals deviations, blocks, and ST &amp; T-wave deviations.</p>
<ol start="8">
<li>Correlate.</li>
</ol>
<p>Are there any physical findings or otherwise that support my impression?</p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div data-testid="stAppIframeResizerAnchor" data-iframe-height="true" class="st-emotion-cache-1dumvfu eht7o1d9"></div></section></div></div></div><div data-testid="portal" id="portal" class="st-emotion-cache-1q6lfs0 e19n7mk11"></div></div><div class=""></div></div>
